Critique of Motion Classification by Pulse Motion
Sanusi critiques the threefold division of motions by pointing out pulse motion as a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pulse motion challenges the threefold division of motions and how it is defined in terms of expansion and contraction.

Core Idea
Pulse motion is a unique counterexample that breaks the classical threefold classification of motions.

In this passage, Sanusi takes on a previous classification that divided all motions into three categories: natural, forced, and psychic. He argues that pulse motion—a vital, rhythmic movement—fits none of these categories. Understanding this critique helps refine our understanding of motion in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the counterexample
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Sanusi begins by pointing out pulse motion as a contradiction to the threefold division.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ينتقض ذلك بحركة النبض'.
2
Understand the threefold division being critiqued
Recall that the threefold division covers natural (up/down), forced (external cause), and psychic (volitional) motion. Sanusi argues pulse motion is none of these.
The critique is that the division claims to exhaust all motions, but pulse motion is an exception.
3
Analyze why pulse motion is not natural, forced, or psychic
The text explains: not natural because it is not up/down; not forced because no external cause; not psychic because not from sensation and will. Confirm each reason from the passage.
The phrase 'ليست شيئاً منهما' is key.
4
Examine the detailed explanation of pulse motion
Sanusi defines contraction and expansion, likening it to a circle of people. Then he explains its purpose: to ventilate the animal spirit. Study the mechanism involving air and the heart.
This part is a detailed description to show pulse motion is a distinct type.
5
Reflect on the critique's significance
Consider how this counterexample strengthens the classification by forcing a refinement. Pulse motion is not a flaw but shows the need for a broader taxonomy.
Sanusi is not rejecting the classification entirely but pointing out its incompleteness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينتقض ذلك ) أعني حصر الحركات في الأقسام الثلاثة المذكورة ( بحركة النبض ) فإنها حركة مؤلفة من انبساط و انقباض لترويح الروح الحيواني بالنسيم. وليست داخلة في الطبيعة مع أن وجه الحصر يقتضي ذلك بظاهره (لأنهم حصروا ) الحركة ( الطبيعية في الصاعدة والهابطة وهي ( أي حركة النبض (ليست شيئاً منهما وكونها ليست إحدى الأخريين (ظاهر إذ ليس حركة النبض صادرة عن شعور وإرادة، ولا عن قوله : (مؤلفة من انبساط و انقباض الانقباض حركة أجزاء العروق من الطرف إلى الوسط، والانبساط حركتها من الوسط إلى الطرف وشبه القدماء ذلك بقوم تحلقوا فيتباعدون مرة إلى خلف فيوسعون دائرتهم ويتقاربون أخرى إلى قدام فيضيقون دائرتهم . قوله : ( لترويح الروح الحيواني) ليس قيداً احترازياً بل هو بيان لغاية حركة النبض، وهي تعديل الروح الحيواني وإخراج فضلاته. وأشار إليهما بقوله : لترويح إلخ . فإنَّ الترويح إنما يحصل بالتعديل والإخراج. وتفصيله أنَّ الروح الحيواني لا يمكن أن يكون إلا لطيفاً حاراً جداً ليكون سريع النفوذ، ولا شك أن اللطيف الحار خصوصاً كثير الحركة يسرع استحالته إلى النار لمناسبة جوهرها وذلك مؤد إلى الاشتعال والخروج عن الآثار النفسانية. فوجب أن يكون لنا جسم بارد مناسب للروح الحيواني في اللطافة والخفة ليعدله وهو الهواء فهو ينفذ إلى القلب والشرايين المتعلقة به بأن يدخل أولاً في الرئة بحركة النفس ثم تدفعة الرئة بعد إصلاحه إلى العروق المسماة بالعروق الخشنة ويندفع منها إلى مسام الشرايين الوريدي ومنها إلى القلب ثم منها إلى البدن ويعدل مزاج الروح الحيواني ثم ذلك الهواء يتسخن بمصاحبة الروح فلا بد من دخول هواء آخر وخروج الأول فيخرج الأول مع الفضلات المفسدة لمزاج الروح.
And that is contradicted, i.e., the confinement of motions to the three mentioned divisions, by the motion of the pulse, for it is a motion composed of expansion and contraction to ventilate the animal spirit with the breeze. And it is not included in nature, although the face of the confinement implies that by its apparent meaning, because they confined natural motion to the ascending and descending, and it (i.e., pulse motion) is neither of them, and it is not one of the other two, evidently, since pulse motion does not issue from sensation and will, nor from... His saying: 'Composed of expansion and contraction'—contraction is the motion of the parts of the veins from the extremity to the center, and expansion is their motion from the center to the extremity. The ancients likened it to a group of people who gather in a circle, then sometimes move backward to widen their circle, and sometimes approach forward to narrow their circle. His saying: 'To ventilate the animal spirit' is not a restrictive condition but is an explanation of the purpose of pulse motion, which is to moderate the animal spirit and expel its wastes. He indicated both by his saying 'to ventilate', etc. For ventilation is achieved only through moderation and expulsion. Its detail is that the animal spirit cannot be but very subtle and hot to be quick in penetration, and there is no doubt that the subtle hot, especially when highly mobile, quickly changes into fire due to the affinity of its essence, and that leads to combustion and departure from psychic effects. So we must have a cold body suitable for the animal spirit in subtlety and lightness to moderate it, and that is air. It penetrates to the heart and the arteries connected to it by entering first into the lungs by the motion of respiration, then the lungs, after modifying it, push it to the veins called rough veins, then it flows from them to the pores of the venous arteries, then to the heart, then from there to the body, and it moderates the temperament of the animal spirit. Then that air becomes heated by accompanying the spirit, so other air must enter and the first must exit, so the first exits with the wastes that corrupt the temperament of the spirit.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the threefold division of motions. It first presents the objection: pulse motion is a counterexample because it is composed of expansion and contraction, intended to ventilate the animal spirit. It is not natural (since natural motion is only up or down), not forced (no external cause), and not psychic (not due to will). Thus, the division is incomplete. The passage then explains pulse motion in detail: contraction moves vein parts from extremity to center, expansion from center to extremity. The purpose is to moderate the animal spirit and expel wastes. The animal spirit is subtle and hot, prone to combustion, so it needs a cool, light body—air—to moderate it. Air enters through respiration, goes to lungs, then to the heart via rough veins, then to the body, moderating the spirit, then exits with wastes. This detailed explanation shows that pulse motion is a distinct type, challenging the classification.

Key Terms
حركة النبض (pulse motion)
الانبساط (expansion)
الانقباض (contraction)
الروح الحيواني (animal spirit)
الترويح (ventilation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pulse motion might be considered natural or psychic motion.
Correction: Pulse motion is neither natural (not up/down), forced (no external cause), nor psychic (not from will). It is a complex vital motion with its own purpose.

Checkpoint
Why does pulse motion contradict the threefold division of motions?
A) It is forced
B) It is psychic
C) It is not natural up/down, not forced, not psychic
D) It is natural upward motion

Key Takeaway
Pulse motion, composed of expansion and contraction to ventilate the animal spirit, does not fit the threefold classification and thus refines our understanding of motion types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own pulse beats every second without your conscious control. This passage reveals it as a precise, divinely ordained mechanism to ventilate your animal spirit. Do you live with such constant awareness of Allah's subtle care in your own body? What specific daily practice can you adopt to turn your awareness to these hidden blessings?
?
The scholars' classification of motions seemed complete, yet pulse motion shattered it. What long-held assumption about your faith or practice are you most resistant to questioning? Ask Allah to reveal one blind spot you have been ignoring, and commit to investigating it with the same rigor Sanusi applied here.
?
Pulse motion is neither natural, forced, nor psychic—it is a unique sign of the Creator's wisdom. If even this small function of your body defies simple categories, how should that affect your humility before divine knowledge? What will you do today to actively surrender your intellectual pride to the One whose knowledge encompasses all things?

The Objection of Pulse Motion and Its Resolution
Al-Iji raises the objection of pulse motion and provides a detailed answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the motion of the pulse (ḥarakat al-nabḍ) does not refute the threefold division of motion when natural motion is properly defined as that which is neither from an external cause nor voluntary.

Core Idea
Pulse motion is an apparent counterexample that actually confirms the threefold division when natural motion is understood broadly.

In this passage, al-Iji confronts a potential objection to the threefold division of motion into natural, forced, and psychic. The objection claims that pulse motion—composed of expansion and contraction—does not fit any category. Al-Iji’s response clarifies the definition of natural motion, showing that pulse motion is indeed natural according to a broader but valid definition. This lesson unpacks the objection, the analysis of pulse motion, and the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening sentences where the objection is raised: pulse motion is not natural (since natural is ascending/descending), not forced (no external cause), and not psychic (not voluntary). How does this challenge the threefold division?
Notice the objection assumes natural motion is strictly ascending/descending.
2
Examine the two possible answers
The author presents two ways to answer the objection. First, if natural motion is confined to ascending/descending, pulse motion is excluded. Second, if natural motion is defined broadly as non-external and non-voluntary, then pulse motion is natural. Which answer does the author favor?
The second definition is the one adopted in kalam.
3
Understand the response to the single-nature objection
A further objection: how can a single nature produce both expansion and contraction? The author replies by analogy with water, which can rise and descend depending on location. This shows that a single nature can produce different effects under different conditions.
The unity of nature does not preclude diverse actions.

قوله : ( ولا يتجه عليه إلخ عطف على فيكون أي إذا كان المراد ذلك لا يتجه عليه أنَّ الطبيعة بالمعنى المراد ها هنا لا يجب أن تكون واحدة حتى يرد عليه ذلك إذ ما لا يكون خارجاً عن المتحرك يجوز أن يكون أموراً متعددة فلا يلزم صدور الأفاعيل المختلفة عن الواحد
قوله : ( في الصاعدة والهابطة أي الصاعدة فقط والهابطة فقط ولذا قال : حركة النبض
ليس شيئاً منهما لأنها مركبة منهما . قوله : ( فإن لم يحصروها فيهما إلخ) قال المصنف في مباحث الحركة : قد أخطأ من جعل الحركة الطبيعية هي الصاعدة والهابطة أو التي على وتيرة واحدة . قوله : (إلا) ما لا يكون خارجاً عن المتحرك إلخ) في العبارة مسامحة والمراد ما لا يكون مبدؤها خارجاً عنه ولا فاعلاً بالإرادة . قوله : ( ولا يتجه عليه أنَّ الطبيعة الواحدة) إذ لا يلزم مما ذكر وحدة الطبيعة لأن المراد بها سبب لا يكون خارجاً عن المتحرك ولا فاعلاً بالإرادة ويجوز أن يكون ذلك السبب متعدداً.
And that [division] is refuted – I mean, the confinement of motions to the three mentioned divisions – by the motion of the pulse (ḥarakat al-nabḍ). For it is a motion composed of expansion (inbisāṭ) and contraction (inqibāḍ) to refresh the animal spirit (al-rūḥ al-ḥayawānī) with the breeze (nasīm). It is not included in the natural (ṭabīʿiyya), while the apparent implication of the confinement necessitates that. Because they confined natural motion to ascending and descending, and it – i.e., pulse motion – is neither of them. And it is clear that it is not one of the other two, since pulse motion does not issue from awareness and will, nor from... [In the OCR, a commentary repeats the earlier definition explanation, then continues:] He said: '(composed of expansion and contraction): contraction is the movement of the parts of the veins from the extremity to the center, and expansion is their movement from the center to the extremity. The ancients likened that to a group of people who form a circle, sometimes moving backward to widen their circle, and other times moving forward to narrow it.' He said: '(to refresh the animal spirit) is not a restrictive qualification but an explanation of the purpose of pulse motion, which is to moderate the animal spirit and expel its wastes. He alluded to both by his saying 'to refresh'. Indeed, refreshing occurs only through moderation and expulsion. The detail: the animal spirit can only be extremely fine and hot to be quick in penetration, and there is no doubt that the fine and hot, especially if highly mobile, quickly transforms into fire due to the suitability of its substance, leading to combustion and departure from psychic effects. Therefore, we must have a cold body suitable for the animal spirit in fineness and lightness to moderate it – that is air. It penetrates to the heart and the arteries connected to it by first entering the lungs through the movement of breathing, then the lungs, after purifying it, push it into the veins called the rough veins, from which it flows into the pores of the venous arteries, then to the heart, then from there to the body, and it moderates the temperament of the animal spirit. Then that air becomes hot by accompanying the spirit, so it is necessary for another air to enter and the first to exit, thus the first exits along with the wastes that corrupt the temperament of the spirit. The inclination of the one who falls wanting his fall is psychic due to the impossibility of holding back; rather, the will must have a role in it. [Page 206] The third observatory – the third objective: on inclination (iʿtimād), which is tendency (mayl). A cause external to the mover. If they do not confine it to the two – i.e., if they do not confine natural to ascending and descending – then pulse motion would be natural, as the implication of the confinement demands, because by 'natural' here we mean only that which is not external to the mover nor an agent by will. Thus the motions of pulse, nutrition, and growth are included in natural motion in the intended sense in this context, as will come. And the objection that a single nature cannot be the source of different effects is not directed against this, until it is answered that the nature of water necessitates its rising and gushing when under the earth and its descending and falling when in the air; so it is possible that the nature of the artery necessitates expansion when...[mention of 'expansion' repeated]. He said: '(If they do not confine it to the two etc.): In Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid, pulse motion is natural, composed of ascending and descending, for the nature of the spirit and arteries is to produce motion from the center to the periphery – expansion – and another from the periphery to the center – contraction. The purpose of reaching the periphery is not to cause stopping and prevent return, but to attract the cool air that corrects the temperament of the spirit; and the purpose of contraction is not to reach the center, but to push out the air that corrupts its temperament. The need for these two things alternates moment by moment, so the effects from a single power alternate.' End of quote. And it is not hidden that the statement that expansion and contraction are ascending and descending motion is far-fetched, for the parts of the blood vessel in both states move from all sides to the center of the vessel or to its extremity. Yes, that statement is valid if it is said that pulse motion is undulating (tawtariyya), according to what some hold. He said: 'If we do not mean etc.' – i.e., we do not mean by it that which issues the action in a uniform manner without awareness and will, as is well-known in contrast to the soul, so that pulse motion would not be natural due to its not being uniform. He said: '(And the objection is not directed against it etc.)' is conjunct to 'thus' – i.e., if the meaning is that, then the objection that nature in the intended sense here does not have to be one is not directed, such that it would be refuted, because that which is not external to the mover may be multiple things, so the issuance of different effects from a single one is not necessary. He said: '(in ascending and descending)' i.e., ascending only and descending only; therefore he said 'pulse motion is neither of them' because it is composed of both. He said: '(If they do not confine it to the two etc.)' The author said in the discussions on motion: 'He who made natural motion the ascending and descending or that which is uniform has erred.' He said: '(except) that which is not external to the mover etc.' – the expression has some latitude, and the meaning is that whose principle is not external to it nor an agent by will. He said: '(And the objection is not directed that a single nature)' because the unity of nature is not necessitated by what was mentioned, since the intended meaning is a cause that is not external to the mover nor an agent by will, and it is possible that that cause be multiple.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. It first presents an objection: the threefold division of motion (natural, forced, psychic) seems to be refuted by pulse motion, because pulse motion is composed of expansion and contraction, is not natural (since natural motion is usually defined as ascending or descending), nor is it forced (since it has no external cause), nor is it psychic (since it is not voluntary). The objection states that pulse motion is not natural because natural is confined to ascending/descending, and it is clearly not one of the other two types. The commentary then elaborates on the nature of pulse motion: expansion and contraction are explained as movements of blood vessel parts to moderate the animal spirit. The detailed explanation of the animal spirit and the role of air shows why pulse motion is necessary for life. The answer on page 206 provides two possible resolutions: either restrict natural motion to ascending/descending only, in which case pulse motion is not natural (but then the objection stands? Actually the text says 'if they do not confine natural to ascending/descending, then pulse motion would be natural'), or define natural motion broadly as any motion that is neither from an external cause nor voluntary. Under this broader definition, pulse motion, along with nutrition and growth, is natural. The commentary then addresses a further objection: how can a single nature produce different effects? It answers by analogy with water, which rises when underground and descends when in air. The nature of the artery can similarly produce both expansion and contraction. The passage also discusses alternative views that pulse motion is undulating. Finally, it clarifies the definition of natural motion and emphasizes that the cause of natural motion may be multiple, not necessarily a single nature. The key idea is that pulse motion does not break the classification when natural motion is properly understood.

Correction: The author shows that when natural motion is defined as 'not from an external cause nor voluntary', pulse motion fits perfectly. The apparent contradiction arises from an overly restrictive definition.

Defining Natural Motion: Including Pulse and Growth
Definition of natural motion and response to a single-nature objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of natural motion as motion whose cause is internal and not by will, and how a single nature can produce different effects.

Core Idea
Natural motion is defined by its internal origin, not its pattern.

This passage broadens the concept of natural motion beyond simple up-and-down movement. The commentator argues that motions like the pulse, nutrition, and growth are natural because their cause is internal to the moving object and not due to will. He then addresses a potential objection: how can a single nature produce multiple, even opposite, motions? The answer is illustrated with the example of water, which behaves differently depending on its environment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the conditional structure: 'if they do not restrict... then...'
The passage uses a hypothetical scenario to expand the definition.
2
Identify the definition of natural motion
The passage states: natural motion is that which is not external to the moving object and not an act of will. Find this key phrase and understand its two conditions.
This definition includes motions like pulse, nutrition, and growth.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection is that a single nature cannot produce different effects. The answer uses water as an example: water's nature causes it to rise when underground and fall when in air. Similarly, the artery's nature can cause both expansion and contraction.
This shows that a single nature can produce varied motions depending on context.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this definition matters in theology and natural philosophy: it allows for a broader category of natural motion, which is important for arguments about divine action and causality.
This connects to the course topic of kalam and the nature of motion.

A cause external to the moving object; so if they do not restrict it to the two [types], i.e., if they do not restrict natural [motion] to the ascending and the descending, then the motion of the pulse would be natural, as required by the manner of restriction. Opinions on Pulse Motion: Forced or Composite?
The commentator discusses opinions on pulse motion and clarifies that the three-fold classification applies only to simple motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different opinions on the nature of pulse motion and why it does not violate the three-fold classification of motion.

Core Idea
Pulse motion is composite, not simple, so it falls outside the three types.

In this passage, the commentator presents several medical and philosophical views on pulse motion. Understanding these helps clarify how the classification of motion into natural, forced, and volitional applies only to simple motions. The pulse, being a composite motion, is not a counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the opinions. Note that it discusses pulse motion as forced by spirit or heart, or composite.
The passage also explains the mechanism behind pulse motion.
2
Identify the three opinions
The passage lists: (1) forced by spirit, (2) forced by heart (with two sub-views), (3) composite. Notice the final clarification.
3
Understand the classification rule
The last sentence states that the three divisions apply only to simple motion. Pulse motion is composite, so it's not a counterexample. This is the key takeaway.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask: Why is this important for kalam? This shows that the classification of motion is robust, and composite motions are not exceptions. It also illustrates how medieval scholars integrated medical knowledge with philosophical categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عرض للروح الذي في جوفه سخونة يحتاج في دفعها إلى جذب هواء صاف والانقباض إذا عرض للهواء المجذوب حرارة وصار كلاً على الروح فيحتاج إلى إخراجه واستبداله بهواء آخر. هذا وقد يقال : إِنَّ حركة النبض قسرية والقاسر هو الروح فإنه يجذب غذاءه الذي هو الهواء ويدفع ما فضل منه فيعرض لوعائه الانبساط بالجذب والانقباض بالدفع. وقيل : القاسر هو القلب إما على سبيل المد والجزر فإنه إذا انبسط القلب توجه إليه الروح من الشرايين فينقبض وإذا انقبض القلب توجه الروح إلى الشرايين فينبسط وأما على سبيل الاستتباع كما تستتبع حركة الشجر حركة أغصانه وفروعه فيكون انبساطها بانبساط القلب وانقباضها بانقباضه وقد يقال أيضاً: إن حركة النبض مركبة، والمنحصر في الأقسام الثلاثة هو الحركة البسيطة فلا نقض بخروجها عنها
It is presented for the spirit which has in its cavity heat needing to repel it by drawing pure air, and contraction when the drawn air becomes hot and becomes a burden on the spirit, so it needs to expel it and replace it with other air. This, and it is said: Indeed the motion of the pulse is forced, and the force is the spirit, for it draws its nourishment which is air and pushes what remains of it, so its vessel undergoes expansion by drawing and contraction by pushing. And it is said: The force is the heart, either by way of ebb and flow, so when the heart expands, the spirit turns toward it from the arteries, so they contract, and when the heart contracts, the spirit turns toward the arteries, so they expand; or by way of accompaniment, as the motion of a tree accompanies the motion of its branches and twigs, so their expansion is by the expansion of the heart and their contraction by its contraction. And it is also said: Indeed the motion of the pulse is composite, and what is confined to the three divisions is simple motion, so there is no objection to its being outside them.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents several views on the nature of pulse motion. It begins by describing the cause: the spirit's heat draws pure air and expels it when it becomes hot and heavy, leading to expansion and contraction. Then it lists opinions: (1) Pulse motion is forced, with the spirit as the force; (2) The heart is the force, either by ebb and flow or by accompaniment; (3) Pulse motion is composite. The passage concludes that the classification of motion into three types (natural, forced, volitional) applies only to simple motion, so composite motion like that of the pulse does not violate this classification.

Correction: The passage shows multiple valid views and emphasizes that pulse motion is composite, and the three-fold classification applies only to simple motion, so pulse motion is not an exception.

Two Rulings of Natural Inclination
Two rulings for the absence of natural inclination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rulings established for the absence of natural inclination in bodies.

Core Idea
A body without natural inclination cannot move at all — not naturally, nor by force or will.

In this passage, the commentator explains the two rulings that scholars have established for a body lacking natural inclination. These rulings show that natural inclination is essential for any kind of motion. Let's examine them carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

Identify the first ruling

3
Identify the second ruling
The second ruling says such a body also cannot move by force or will. The argument begins but the text is cut off; nevertheless, the ruling stands.
The passage is incomplete, but the doctrine is clear.

أما الميل الطبيعي فأثبتوا له حكمين الأول : أنَّ العادم له (أي للميل الطبيعي بل لمبدئه) لا يتحرك بالطبع وهو ظاهر إذ لا معنى للحركة الطبيعية إلا ما مبدؤها القريب هو الميل الطبيعي ولا يتحرك أيضاً بالقسر والإرادة إذ لو تحرك العادم لمبدأ الميل الطبيعي بقوة

The passage underscores that the absence of natural inclination renders a body completely inert with respect to all three categories of motion.

The Conscious Coercer Objection
Objection on conscious coercer in compulsory motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim about compulsory motion requiring an obstacle is restricted when the coercer has consciousness.

Core Idea
A conscious coercer can determine speed, making the motion effectively voluntary.

In this passage, an objection is raised against the earlier argument that compulsory motion always needs an obstacle to determine its speed. The objection points out that if the coercer has consciousness, he can determine the speed by his own will. The author then shows how the claim must be restricted to cases where the coercer lacks consciousness, aligning the motion with voluntary motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the objection and response unfold in the author's words.
2
Identify the objection
Find the sentence where the objection is introduced (the part beginning with 'there is an objection'). Notice the key point: the coercer may have consciousness.
3
Understand the restriction
The answer acknowledges the objection and restricts the original claim: it applies only when the coercer lacks consciousness. Recognize that this preserves the inference for unconscious coercers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن كانت الحركة طبيعية أو (قسرية) الظاهر من سياق كلامه أن حاصل الاستدلال أنه يلزم من انتفاء واحد من المعاوق الداخلي والخارجي في الحركة القسرية ومن انتفاء المعاوق الخارجي في الحركة الطبيعية أن لا تتحقق حركة أصلاً أو يخلو الحركة عن لازمها. أعني حداً من السرعة والبطء لعدم تحقق ما يحددهما حينئذ وفيه أنَّ القاسر ربما كان ذا شعور فيتحدد حال الحركة بإرادته فلا يثبت السبب الكلي . نعم لو استلزم جواز الحركة القسرية في الجملة جوازها في جميع الصور تم الاستدلال للزوم المحال في بعض الصور أعني فيما إذا لم يكن القاسر ذا شعور لكن أين ذلك الاستلزام مع ظهور الفارق ثم إِنَّ التقرير المذكور لا يلائم قوله : حتى يمكن استناد الحدود المختلفة إلخ . ولا قوله : لم يقع بسببه تفاوت لإشعارهما بأن حاصل الاستدلال امتناع صدور الحدود المختلفة من الطبيعة والقاسر. والظاهر أنَّ لا مخلص إلا بتخصيص الدعوى بما إذا لم يكن القاسر ذا شعور وأما إذا كان ذا شعور فالحركة القسرية في حكم الحركة الإرادية .
His saying: 'If the motion is natural or compulsory' — the apparent from the context of his speech is that the gist of the inference is that from the absence of one of the internal or external obstacles in compulsory motion, and from the absence of the external obstacle in natural motion, it necessarily follows that either no motion occurs at all or the motion lacks its necessary concomitant, namely a limit of speed and slowness, due to the non-existence of what determines them. There is an objection: the coercer may sometimes have consciousness, so the state of motion is determined by his will, so the universal cause is not established. Indeed, if the permissibility of compulsory motion in general necessitated its permissibility in all instances, the inference would be conclusive because of the impossibility of some instances, namely when the coercer does not have consciousness. But where is that necessity given the evident difference? Moreover, the aforementioned reasoning does not accord with his saying 'so that the different limits could be based on it' nor with his saying 'no variation occurs because of it', since both indicate that the gist of the inference is the impossibility of different limits proceeding from nature and the coercer. And it appears that there is no escape except by restricting the claim to cases where the coercer does not have consciousness; but if he has consciousness, then compulsory motion is in the same ruling as voluntary motion.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the earlier claim that natural and compulsory motion always need an obstacle. The objection argues that if the coercer (القاسر) has consciousness (شعور), he can determine the speed by his own will, so the previous argument fails to establish a universal cause. The response acknowledges this and restricts the claim: the reasoning applies only when the coercer lacks consciousness. If the coercer is conscious, the motion is effectively voluntary and does not need an obstacle. The commentator also notes that the original wording of the base text implies that the inference is about the impossibility of different limits arising from nature and the coercer, which further necessitates this restriction.

Key Terms
قاسر
معاوق داخلي
معاوق خارجي
حركة قسرية
حركة إرادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All compulsory motion must have an obstacle to determine its speed.
Correction: Compulsory motion caused by a conscious coercer does not need an obstacle because the coercer's will determines the speed, making it like voluntary motion.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection addressed in this passage?
A) Natural motion can also be determined by the soul
B) The coercer may have consciousness and thus can determine the speed, weakening the claim
C) The obstacle is always necessary even in voluntary motion
D) The internal obstacle is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The claim about compulsory motion requiring an obstacle applies only when the coercer lacks consciousness; otherwise, the motion is voluntary and self-determined.

Nature's Consciousness: Objection and Answer
Objection on nature's consciousness and its resolution by distinguishing positive from variation-causing consciousness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosopher distinguishes between two types of consciousness in nature and why negating consciousness does not contradict the claim that nature has a positive tendency.

Core Idea
Nature has a positive tendency, not free choice; that is the intended consciousness.

This passage addresses a crucial objection in the philosophy of nature: if nature is said to have no consciousness, how can it be that earlier philosophers attributed some consciousness to it? The answer hinges on a distinction between consciousness that causes variation and mere positive awareness. Let's follow the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection that cites Ibn Sina's Commentary on the Allusions, claiming nature has consciousness. Understand the apparent contradiction.
2
Understand the answer
The answer clarifies that the intended negation is of consciousness that necessitates difference in motion. Nature acts by necessity, not choice.
Notice the example of a stone falling to its natural place.
3
Grasp the distinction
Nature has positive consciousness (شعور إيجابي) — an inherent tendency toward a goal, not a voluntary decision causing variation.
This resolves the contradiction.
4
Apply the concept
Consider how this distinction applies to other cases like coercive motion and the receiver of motion. Reflect on why variation doesn't occur without voluntary elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأن الطبيعة لا شعور لها قيل عليه : قد صرح في النمط الرابع من شرح الإشارات بأنَّ للطبيعة شعوراً ما، فسلب الشعور عنها ينافيه وأجيب بأنَّ المراد الشعور الموجب لاختلاف الحركة فإن الطبيعة تحريكها بطريق الإيجاب لا بالاختيار ضرورة أن الحجر لا يمكن أن لا يتحرك للحركة إليها بل هي بحسب ذاتها تطلب الحصول في فتكاد المكان الطبيعي تقتضي المسافة في غير زمان لو أمكن. وكذا القاسر إذا فرض تحريكه بقوة واحدة لم يقع بسببه تفاوت والقابل للحركة أعني الجسم المتحرك لا تفاوت فيه إذا لم يكن فيه الإرادي الذي يترتب عليه الاختلاف في الأفعال فلا ينافي ما صرح به ذلك البعض من أن الطبيعة لها شعور فإنه أثبت الشعور الإيجابي ولذا قال : حتى يمكن إلخ .
His saying: 'Because nature has no consciousness' — it was objected against him: It was stated in the Fourth Pattern of the Commentary on the Allusions (Sharh al-Isharat) that nature has some consciousness, so negating consciousness from it contradicts that. And the answer is that what is intended is the consciousness that necessitates difference in motion, for nature moves in a necessitating way, not by choice, necessarily because a stone cannot not move toward its natural place; rather, it inherently seeks to arrive there, so the natural place necessitates the distance in non-time if possible. Similarly, the coercer, if his motion is assumed to be with a single force, no variation occurs because of him; and the receiver of motion, i.e., the moving body, has no variation in it if it does not have the voluntary [element] that results in difference in actions. So it does not contradict what that person stated that nature has consciousness, for he affirmed positive consciousness, and therefore he said: 'so that [the different limits] could be based on it, etc.'
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the claim that nature has no consciousness (شعور). The objector cites Ibn Sina's Commentary on the Allusions (شرح الإشارات) where nature is said to have some consciousness. The answer clarifies that what is negated is the kind of consciousness that would cause variation in motion; nature does not choose but acts by necessity (إيجاب). Nature inherently seeks its natural place, aiming for the fastest possible motion. Similarly, a coercer applying a constant force produces no variation. The body itself lacks variation unless it has voluntary elements. Thus, nature's positive awareness (شعور إيجابي) is not the type that would determine different speeds, so the objection is resolved.

Key Terms
شعور
شعور إيجابي
طبيعة
إشارات
إيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That nature has no awareness at all.
Correction: Nature has a positive tendency toward its goal, though not freely choosing.

Checkpoint
How does the answer distinguish the type of consciousness nature has?
A) Nature has voluntary consciousness
B) Nature has consciousness that causes variation in motion
C) Nature has positive consciousness (tendency) but not the kind that causes variation
D) Nature has no consciousness at all

Key Takeaway
Nature has a positive consciousness (inherent tendency) but not a voluntary, variation-causing consciousness, thus resolving the apparent contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you find yourself acting more by 'necessary habit' (like nature's positive tendency) or by deliberate, conscious choice? What is one area where you are merely 'falling' like the stone rather than choosing your path?
?
If nature's 'consciousness' is only an inherent inclination without free will, what does that imply about your own responsibility? Are there aspects of your character that you treat as unchangeable 'nature' when in fact you have the free will to change them?
?
The lesson shows that apparent contradictions are resolved by distinguishing types of consciousness. Where in your spiritual life have you assumed a contradiction (e.g., between predestination and effort) that might be resolved by a similar distinction? Will you act on that insight today?

Nature and the Coercer Seek Fastest Motion
Explains that both nature and a perfect coercer seek fastest motion, requiring an obstacle for variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why natural motion and compelled motion by a uniform force tend towards the fastest possible, and why an obstacle is needed to determine their speed.

Core Idea
Nature and perfect coercion both seek instantaneous motion; obstacles create variation.

This passage explores the underlying principle of motion: nature, left to itself, would move as fast as possible. Similarly, a coercer applying uniform force without intent would also produce instantaneous motion. The commentator explains that without an obstacle, no variation in speed occurs, and he clarifies the scope of this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the first part on nature's tendency
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the commentator describes nature's inclination to the fastest motion, almost instantaneous.
This sets up the parallel with the coercer.
2
Understand the parallel with the coercer
See how the coercer, when applying a single force without intent, also tends to produce instantaneous motion, mirroring nature.
3
Identify the need for an obstacle
The text states that to determine speed, an obstacle (معاوق) is required. Without it, motion would be instantaneous.
This applies only to the specific case of a coercer without will.
4
Clarify the exception
Note that not all compulsory motions need an obstacle; those from a coercer with will are like voluntary motions.
This resolves the objection about the three states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل هي بحسب ذاتها تطلب إلخ إنما تطلب الحركة بواسطة أنه لا يمكن الوصول الاستدلال بدونها فهي تطلب أسرع الحركات التي تكاد تقع في آن . قوله : ( وكذلك القاسر أي احتاج في تحديد حالها من الإسراع والإبطاء إلى معاوق إذا فرض تحريك القاسر بقوة واحدة أي لا اختلاف فيها بالشدة والضعف بأن يوجدها القاسر في الجسم من غير قصد إلى مرتبة من مراتبها لكون مقصوده حصول ذلك الجسم من مكان فيكون القاسر على أتم ما يمكن أن يكون فلا يقع بسببه تفاوت أيضاً بل يكاد أن يحصل المقسور في المكان القسري في آن لو أمكن كالطبيعة ثم إنه لا دلالة في التخصيص بالحركتين على جواز بجميع أفرادهما فلا خلل في خروج القسرية التي مبدؤها قاسر. وإرادة على أنها في حكم الإرادية وعبارة ذلك البعض أوضح وأخصر فإنه قال : والقاسر إذا فرض على أتم ما يمكن أن يكون لا يقع أيضاً بسببه تفاوت وبما حررنا لك اندفع ما قيل: إنه إذا لم يكن بسبب القاسر تفاوت يكون الزمان الذي اقتضاه القاسر محفوظاً في الأحوال الثلاث والزمان بسبب المعاوق منقسماً بحسب انقسامه فلا يلزم أن يكون الحركة مع العائق كهي لا معه، وذلك لأن مقصوده أنَّ القاسر لا يمكن أن يحدد السرعة والبطء إذا فرض على أتم ما يمكن لا أنه تحدد مع الأحوال الثلاث على أن كلامه ليس مبنيا على فرض القاسر في الأحوال الثلاث بل على القاسر في نفسه لا يمكن أن يكون محدداً . قوله : ( والقابل للحركة إلخ هذا زائد على كلام البعض يعني أن الجسم من حيث إنه جسم قابل للحركة مطلقاً وليس فيه تحديد لمرتبة من مراتبها وإلا لكانت تلك لازمة للجسمية في جميع الأحوال غير قابلة لمرتبة أخرى بل التفاوت إنما يكون فيه بحسب المعاوق الداخلي أو [الاستواء؟] أنَّ إلى أسفل فلا يتصور ان يختلف اقتضاؤها وبهذا التقرير اندفع ما قيل من أنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون للطبيعة درجة مخصوصة من الحركة خصوصية تقتضيها لأجلها كاقتضائها البرودة المخصوصة أو الحرارة المخصوصة أو غيرهما من الأعراض القابلة للتفاوت، ووجه الاندفاع ظاهر على أن مقتضى الطبيعة ليس إلا الحصول في المكان الطبيعي ولا يقتضي الحركة إلا لأجل هذا الحصول فيكاد يقتضي قطع المسافة في آن لو أمكن. فحينئذ لا يعقل أن يكون للطبيعة خصوصية مع درجة من الحركة إلا مع حركة لا يمكن أسرع منها وتلك الحركة غير ممكنة كما سبق في بحث الخلاء في تحقيق أن القوى الجسمانية لا يجوز أن تكون غير متناهية في الشدة. نعم يرد عليه ما أورده الشارح هناك .
His saying: 'Rather, by its essence it seeks, etc.' — It only seeks motion because it cannot arrive without it, so it seeks the fastest motions that almost occur in an instant. His saying: 'And likewise the coercer' — i.e., it needs an obstacle to determine its state of acceleration and deceleration if the coercer's motion is assumed to be with a single force, i.e., no variation in intensity and weakness, because the coercer produces it in the body without intending a particular degree, since his purpose is only to transfer the body from a place; so the coercer is at the utmost possible, so no variation occurs because of him either, but rather the coerced almost arrives at the forced place in an instant if possible, like nature. Moreover, there is no indication in the specification of the two types of motion that all individuals of these types are permissible, so there is no defect in excluding the compulsory motion whose source is a coercer with will, for it is in the ruling of voluntary motion. And the expression of that person is clearer and more concise, for he said: 'And the coercer, if assumed at the utmost possible, no variation occurs because of him either.' And with what we have written for you, the objection that was raised is repelled: that if there is no variation due to the coercer, then the time that the coercer necessitated would be preserved in the three states, and the time due to the obstacle would be divided according to its division, so it does not necessarily follow that motion with the obstacle is like motion without it. That is because his purpose is that the coercer cannot determine speed and slowness if assumed at the utmost possible, not that it is determined with the three states. In fact, his speech is not based on assuming the coercer in the three states, but on the coercer in himself not being able to be a determiner. His saying: 'And the receiver of motion, etc.' — this is additional to the speech of that person, meaning that the body, insofar as it is a body, is absolutely receptive to motion and has no determination of a particular degree; otherwise, that degree would be necessary for bodiliness in all states and not receptive to another degree; rather, variation occurs in it only according to the internal obstacle or [the level?] that ... so it cannot be conceived that its requirement differs. And by this summary, what was said is repelled: 'Why is it not permissible for nature to have a specific degree of motion as a particular property that necessitates it, just as it necessitates specific coldness or heat or other divisible accidents?' The repulsion is apparent because the requirement of nature is only arrival at the natural place, and it does not require motion except for this arrival; thus, it almost requires covering the distance in an instant if possible. Hence, it is inconceivable for nature to have a specific property with a degree of motion except along with a motion than which none is faster, and that motion is impossible, as previously discussed in the examination of void in the investigation that bodily powers cannot be infinite in intensity. Yes, there is an objection against it from what the commentator (al-Sharif) presented there.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the nature of natural motion: nature seeks the fastest possible motion, almost instantaneous (في آن). Similarly, a coercer using a constant force without intending a specific degree also tends to produce instantaneous motion. The commentator defends the earlier restriction, noting that the claim of needing an obstacle does not apply to all compulsory motions—only where the coercer lacks will. He then refutes an objection about time and obstacles, clarifying that the coercer's inability to determine speed is absolute, not relative to states. Next, he adds that the moving body itself has no inherent speed determination; variation comes from obstacles. Finally, he addresses an objection: why cannot nature have a specific degree of motion (e.g., specific speed) like it has specific coldness? The answer is that nature's sole end is reaching the natural place, so it would demand the fastest possible motion, which is impossible (since bodily powers cannot be infinite in intensity). Thus, nature cannot have a specific degree; any such degree would imply a faster impossible motion. The passage concludes by noting an objection from the commentator (al-Sharif) elsewhere.

قاسر
حركة سريعة
آن
معاوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All compulsory motions require an obstacle to have any variation in speed.
Correction: Only those from a coercer without will; motions from a coercer with will are like voluntary and can vary from intention, so they do not need an obstacle.

Checkpoint
Why does a coercer with a single uniform force need an obstacle to produce variation in speed?
A) Because the coercer's force varies over time
B) Because without an obstacle the motion would be instantaneous
C) Because the coercer intends specific speeds that require obstacles
D) Because nature always resists the coercer

Key Takeaway
Nature and a perfect coercer both tend toward instantaneous motion; only obstacles create differences in speed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see that your own nature seeks the fastest, easiest path. In your journey to Allah, where are you letting obstacles—whether laziness, distractions, or doubts—slow you down? Will you remove them today, or let them continue to hinder you?
?
If a perfect coercer produces no variation, what does that say about the variations in your spiritual states? Could it be that your own intentions (like a coercer with will) are the real cause of your ups and downs? Reflect on one area where you have been inconsistent and commit to a more constant effort.
?
The passage says that without an obstacle, motion would be instantaneous. What 'obstacles' have you placed between yourself and the pursuit of truth? Is it fear, comfort, or pride? Identify one such obstacle and make a plan to confront it this week.

Refuting the Claim of a Specific Degree for Nature
Refutes the idea that nature has a specific degree of motion, using the body's receptivity and the impossibility of infinite speed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nature cannot have a specific inherent degree of motion, unlike other accidents such as coldness.

Core Idea
Nature's sole goal is reaching its natural place, so it demands the fastest possible motion, making a specific degree impossible.

The commentator extends the argument by stating that the body itself has no inherent speed; speed variations come from obstacles. He then tackles a key objection: why cannot nature have a specific speed as a property, just as it has specific coldness? The answer lies in nature's purpose: only the fastest motion would fulfill it, but such motion is impossible, so nature cannot be tied to any finite degree.

Guided Path
1
Understand the body's receptivity
The text says the body, as such, is absolutely receptive to motion with no inherent limit or specific degree. Otherwise, that degree would be necessary for bodiliness at all times.
This sets up the argument against a specific degree.
2
Note variation comes from obstacles
Variation in speed occurs only due to internal or external obstacles (معاوق), not from the body itself.
3
Grasp the objection
The objection asks: why cannot nature have a specific degree of motion as a property, just as it has specific coldness or heat?
This is a common and strong objection.
4
Follow the refutation
The commentator refutes: nature's requirement is only arrival at the natural place; it does not require motion except for that arrival. Hence, it would demand the fastest possible motion (instantaneous). Since such motion is impossible (as bodily powers cannot be infinite), no specific finite degree is possible.
This relies on earlier discussions about the impossibility of infinite intensity in bodily powers.

Ideal Natural and Compulsive Motion: Instantaneity and the Objection Refuted
Commentary on natural and compulsive motion instantaneity and objection refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ideal natural and compulsive motion would be instantaneous, and how the objection regarding the compeller's role in determining time is refuted.

Core Idea
In ideal conditions, both natural and compulsive motion would be instantaneous; only obstacles cause variation.

In this passage, the commentator explains that when a mover (nature or compeller) operates at its utmost perfection, the resulting motion would occur in an instant if no obstacle existed. This leads to an objection about the compeller's ability to determine time, which is then clarified by showing that the compeller itself cannot be a determiner of speed or slowness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the commentator builds the argument.
The passage is a commentary on a previous text; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the two claims about instantaneity
Find where the commentator states that natural motion would occur in an instant, and similarly that compulsive motion would occur in an instant if the compeller is at utmost perfection.
Look for phrases like 'تكاد تقع في آن' and 'يكاد أن يحصل المقسور في المكان القسري في آن'.
3
Understand the objection and its refutation
Read the objection: if the compeller causes no variation, time would be the same in all states, but the obstacle divides time. Then see how the commentator refutes it by clarifying that the compeller cannot determine speed at all.
The key point is that the compeller itself is not a determiner; the obstacle alone determines variation.

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل للحركة إليها بل هي بحسب ذاتها تطلب الحصول في فتكاد المكان الطبيعي تقتضي المسافة في غير زمان لو أمكن. وكذا القاسر إذا فرض تحريكه بقوة واحدة لم [يقع] بسببه تفاوت والقابل للحركة أعني الجسم المتحرك لا تفاوت فيه إذا لم يكن فيه الإرادي الذي يترتب عليه الاختلاف في الأفعال فلا ينافي ما صرح به ذلك البعض من أن الطبيعة لها شعور فإنه أثبت الشعور الإيجابي ولذا قال : حتى يمكن إلخ . قوله : (بل هي بحسب ذاتها تطلب إلخ إنما تطلب الحركة بواسطة أنه لا يمكن الوصول الاستدلال بدونها فهي تطلب أسرع الحركات التي تكاد تقع في آن . قوله : ( وكذلك القاسر أي احتاج في تحديد حالها من الإسراع والإبطاء إلى معاوق إذا فرض تحريك القاسر بقوة واحدة أي لا اختلاف فيها بالشدة والضعف بأن يوجدها القاسر في الجسم من غير قصد إلى مرتبة من مراتبها لكون مقصوده حصول ذلك الجسم من مكان فيكون القاسر على أتم ما يمكن أن يكون فلا يقع بسببه تفاوت أيضاً بل يكاد أن يحصل المقسور في المكان القسري في آن لو أمكن كالطبيعة ثم إنه لا دلالة في التخصيص بالحركتين على جواز بجميع أفرادهما فلا خلل في خروج القسرية التي مبدؤها قاسر. وإرادة على أنها في حكم الإرادية وعبارة ذلك البعض أوضح وأخصر فإنه قال : والقاسر إذا فرض على أتم ما يمكن أن يكون لا يقع أيضاً بسببه تفاوت وبما حررنا لك اندفع ما قيل: إنه إذا لم يكن بسبب القاسر تفاوت يكون الزمان الذي اقتضاه القاسر محفوظاً في الأحوال الثلاث والزمان بسبب المعاوق منقسماً بحسب انقسامه فلا يلزم أن يكون الحركة مع العائق كهي لا معه، وذلك لأن مقصوده أنَّ القاسر لا يمكن أن يحدد السرعة والبطء إذا فرض على أتم ما يمكن لا أنه تحدد مع الأحوال الثلاث على أن كلامه ليس مبنيا على فرض القاسر في الأحوال الثلاث بل على القاسر في نفسه لا يمكن أن يكون محدداً .
The Third Observatory - The Third Aim: On i'timad (dependence), which is inclination (mayl). Motion towards it, but it (nature) in itself seeks attainment, so natural place nearly requires distance in non-time if possible. Likewise the compeller (qasir) if his moving with one force is assumed, no variation occurs because of him. And the recipient of motion, i.e. the moving body, has no variation in it if there is no voluntary [action] which entails difference in acts. So it does not contradict what that some (scholar) stated that nature has perception, for he affirmed positive perception. Hence he said: 'until possible, etc.' His saying: 'But it in itself seeks, etc.' It only seeks motion by means of the fact that arrival is not possible without it, so it seeks the fastest motions that nearly occur in an instant. His saying: 'And likewise the compeller' i.e. it needed in determining its state of speed and slowness an obstacle (mu'awiq) if the compeller's moving with one force is assumed, i.e. no variation in it in intensity and weakness, by the compeller producing it in the body without intending a rank of its ranks, because his aim is the attainment of that body from a place, so the compeller is at the utmost possible, so no variation occurs because of him either, but rather the compelled (maqsūr) nearly reaches the compulsive place in an instant if possible, like nature. Then there is no indication in the specification of the two motions for permission of all their individuals, so no defect in excluding the compulsive whose origin is a compeller. And it is meant that it is in the ruling of voluntary. And the expression of that some (scholar) is clearer and more concise, for he said: 'And the compeller if assumed at the utmost possible, no variation also occurs because of him.' And by what we have refined for you, what was said is refuted: 'If there is no variation due to the compeller, the time required by the compeller is preserved in the three states, and the time due to the obstacle is divided according to its division, so it does not follow that the motion with the obstacle is like it without it.' That is because his purpose is that the compeller cannot determine speed and slowness if assumed at the utmost possible, not that it determines with the three states; rather his statement is not based on assuming the compeller in the three states, but on the compeller in itself cannot be a determiner.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a previous text discussing natural and compulsive motion. It begins by stating that natural motion, by its nature, seeks to reach its natural place so urgently that it would cross the distance in no time if possible. Similarly, if a compeller (qasir) applies a single force without any variation in intensity and without intending any particular speed, the resulting motion would also be instantaneous if no obstacle exists. The commentator then addresses an objection: if the compeller itself causes no variation, then the time required would be the same in all circumstances (with obstacle, without obstacle, etc.), and the obstacle alone divides time, so the motion with obstacle would not equal motion without it. The refutation clarifies that the compeller cannot determine speed or slowness at all when assumed at the utmost perfection; it is not that it determines a specific time in each state. The discussion underscores that variation in speed arises from obstacles, not from the mover's inherent nature when perfectly efficient.

Key Terms
الاعتماد (i'timad)
الميل (mayl)
الحركة الطبيعية (natural motion)
القاسر (compeller)
المعاوق (obstacle)
آن (instant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the compeller or nature causes no variation, then motion with and without an obstacle would be the same, as the time would be preserved.
Correction: The point is that the compeller itself cannot determine any speed or slowness; it is the obstacle that is the sole determiner of variation. Therefore, the objection's assumption that the compeller preserves a constant time is mistaken.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a compeller is at its utmost perfection, what causes variation in speed?
A) The compeller's intention
B) The nature of the moving body
C) The obstacle (mu'awiq)
D) The distance traveled

Key Takeaway
The speed of motion is determined by obstacles, not by the mover when it is at utmost perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your spiritual journey should be instantaneous if you only had the right 'force'? Where do you blame delays on obstacles rather than seeing them as the very means by which Allah determines your pace and purifies your reliance on Him?
?
When you face a trial that slows you down, do you resent the obstacle, or do you accept that it is the only thing shaping your speed? How does this truth call you to trust that every delay is precisely what you need right now?

Causality and Created Things

The Need for an Obstacle and Its Classification
Establishes the need for an obstacle to determine motion speed and classifies obstacles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an obstacle is necessary to determine speed in motion and how obstacles are classified into internal and external, with external being the consistency of the medium.

Core Idea
Speed is not inherent in bodies; it requires an obstacle to determine it.

The commentator explains that a body, as a recipient of motion, has no built-in speed. The variation in speed comes from obstacles, either inside or outside the moving object. The passage clarifies the nature of external obstacles and refutes alternative suggestions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the necessity of an obstacle and how it determines speed.
2
Identify the classification
Find where the author mentions internal and external obstacles. Understand that the external obstacle is the consistency of the medium.
3
Understand the objection and response
Note the objection about time being preserved in three states, and see how the commentator dismisses it.
4
Grasp the determination of inclination
Understand how differences in the body (size, density, position) determine the rank of inclination, not nature or compeller.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والقابل للحركة إلخ هذا زائد على كلام البعض يعني أن الجسم من حيث إنه جسم قابل للحركة مطلقاً وليس فيه تحديد لمرتبة من مراتبها وإلا لكانت تلك لازمة للجسمية في جميع الأحوال غير قابلة لمرتبة أخرى بل التفاوت إنما يكون فيه بحسب المعاوق الداخلي أو الخارجي وقد أورد على هذا مثل ما أورد على القاسر بأنه إذا لم يكن تفاوت بسببه كان ذلك الزمان محفوظاً في الأحوال الثلاث فلا يتم الاستدلال وأنت خبير بعدم وروده على ما حررناه . قوله: (إذ لو لم يعاوقه إلخ) لأنه على تقدير عدم المعاوق، إما أن لا يكون له تعلق بالحركة أو يكون له تعلق بالإعانة وعلى التقديرين لا يكون محدداً أما على الأول فظاهر وأما على الثاني فلأنه إذا كان مقتضى الطبيعة والقاسر أقصى مراتب الإسراع لا يتصور الإعاقة فيه وأما ما قيل : إنَّ الأمر الآخر لا يلزم أن يكون معاوقاً، بل نقول ذلك الأمر هو الميل على ما صرح به ذلك البعض فمدفوع بأن ذلك الأمر المعاوق إنما يكون تحديده لحد من السرعة والبطء بتحديده أو لا مرتبة من مراتب الميل فإن الطبيعية أو القاسر لا يعينان مرتبة من مراتب الميل، وإنما يتعين باختلاف الجسم ذي الطبيعة في الكم أي الصغر والكبر والكيف أي التخلخل والتكاثف أو الوضع أي اندماج الأجزاء وانتفاشها أو بحسب رقة ما فيه الحركة وغلظه وبما ذكرنا اندفع التدافع بين كلامي ذلك البعض حيث قال : إنَّ المحدد للسرعة والبطء هو المعاوق وصرح قبيل هذا البيان بأنه الميل . قوله : ( فالخارج) هو قوام إلخ ) لأن ما سوى المسافة والمحرك والمتحرك من الأمور الخارجة لا يلزم الحركة فلا يمكن أن يكون محدداً لما يلزمها من السرعة والبطء فاندفع ما قيل: لم لا أن يكون أمر آخر غير القوام كالقوة الجاذبة للمغناطيس مثلاً محدداً بحسب اختلافها في القوة والضعف . قوله : ( ولا يتصور في الحركة الطبيعية معاوق داخلي إلخ) أي إذا كانت في الأجسام البسيطة لأنه لا يكون ذلك المعاوق حينئذ إلا الطبيعة فاندفع ما قيل : ما ذكره من قوله : لاستحالة إنما يدل على كون الطبيعة معاوقاً والمعاوق الداخلي أعم منها فيجوز أن يكون نفساً كالطير الساقط من عدم مكان هو يطير إليه . قوله : ( فالخارج هو قوام ما في المسافة قيل : لا نسلم ذلك لم لا يجوز أن يكون أمراً آخر غير القوام كالقوة الجاذبة للمغناطيس مثلاً فإنا لو أخذنا بيدنا قطعة من المغناطيس مع الحديد ثم أرسلنا الحديد فإنه يتحرك بالطبع إلى أسفل ويعاوقه في الحركة قوة المغناطيس ويتسارع في الحركة بحسب تباعده من المغناطيس قوله : ( ولا يتصور في الحركة الطبيعية معاوق داخلي هذا في حركات البسائط وأما في حركات المركبات الطبيعية فيمكن فيها المعاوقة الداخلية من أجزاء مادية والسر فيه أن حركة
His saying: 'And the recipient of motion, etc.' This is additional to the statement of that some (scholar), meaning that the body insofar as it is a body is receptive to motion absolutely and there is in it no determination of a rank of its ranks; otherwise that would be necessary for corporeality in all states, not susceptible to another rank. Rather variation occurs in it only according to internal or external obstacle. And an objection was raised against this like that raised against the compeller: if there is no variation due to it, that time is preserved in the three states, so the inference is not complete. But you are aware that it does not apply to what we have refined. His saying: 'For if it did not obstruct him, etc.' Because on the assumption of no obstacle, either it has no connection to motion or it has connection by assistance; in both cases it is not a determiner. As for the first, it is obvious. As for the second, because if the requirement of nature and the compeller is the utmost ranks of speed, assistance in it is inconceivable. And as for what was said: 'The other thing does not have to be an obstacle; rather we say that thing is inclination (mayl),' as that some (scholar) stated, it is refuted because that obstacle thing's determination of a limit of speed and slowness is by its determining a rank of the ranks of inclination; for nature or the compeller do not specify a rank of ranks of inclination; rather it is specified by the difference of the body having nature in quantity (smallness and largeness) and quality (rarefaction and condensation) or position (integration of parts and their expansion) or according to the fineness of what is in it of motion and its coarseness. And by what we mentioned, the contradiction between the two statements of that some (scholar) is refuted, where he said: 'The determiner of speed and slowness is the obstacle,' and he stated before this explanation that it is inclination. His saying: 'So the external' is the consistency, etc., because besides distance, mover, and moved, external things do not necessitate motion, so they cannot be a determiner for what accompanies it of speed and slowness. Thus what was said is refuted: 'Why not be something else besides consistency, like the magnetic attractive force, for example, a determiner according to its difference in strength and weakness?' His saying: 'And an internal obstacle is inconceivable in natural motion, etc.' i.e., when in simple bodies, because then that obstacle is only nature. Thus what was said is refuted: 'What he mentioned from his saying: 'due to impossibility' only indicates that nature is an obstacle, and the internal obstacle is more general than it, so it may be a soul, like a bird falling from a place it is flying to.' His saying: 'So the external is the consistency of what is in the distance.' It was said: 'We do not accept that; why not allow something else besides consistency, like the magnetic attractive force? For if we take in our hand a piece of magnet with iron and then release the iron, it moves naturally downwards and the magnet's force opposes it in motion and it accelerates according to its distance from the magnet.' His saying: 'And an internal obstacle is inconceivable in natural motion.' This is in the motions of simples, but in the motions of natural composites, internal obstruction from material parts is possible. The secret is that motion...
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on motion. It begins by explaining that the body itself, as a recipient of motion, has no inherent determination of speed; variation comes only from an obstacle, either internal or external. An objection is raised similar to one against the compeller: if the body itself causes no variation, time would be the same in all states. The commentator dismisses it. He then argues that without an obstacle, the obstacle has either no relation to motion or only assists; in neither case can it determine speed. A further objection suggests that the determining factor might be inclination (mayl), not an obstacle. The response is that the obstacle determines speed by determining a rank of inclination, since nature or compeller do not specify a rank; rather, it is specified by differences in the body (size, density, position, etc.). The commentator reconciles two statements of a previous scholar: one saying the obstacle is the determiner, another saying it is inclination. Next, he argues that the external obstacle is the consistency of the medium in the distance, and he refutes the possibility of another external factor like magnetic force, because only the medium necessarily accompanies motion. Regarding internal obstacles, he states they are inconceivable in simple natural bodies because nature itself would be the obstacle; an objection that a soul (like a bird) could be an internal obstacle is refuted by noting that this applies only to composite bodies, not simples. The passage ends with a mention that internal obstruction is possible in composites, but the text cuts off.

القابل للحركة
المعاوق
المعاوق الداخلي
المعاوق الخارجي
الميل
القوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The body itself determines its speed of motion.
Correction: The body is receptive to motion without inherent determination; speed variation comes only from obstacles.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines the rank of inclination (mayl) in natural motion?
A) The nature of the mover
B) The compeller's force
C) The differences in the body (size, density, position)
D) The external obstacle alone

Key Takeaway
The obstacle is essential to determine speed, and external obstacle is the medium's consistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the speed of your spiritual progress, what 'obstacles' have you been ignoring? Are you assuming your intention alone (like inclination) is enough, or have you considered the external factors (environment, habits) that determine your pace?
?
The passage teaches that variation comes from obstacles, not from the body's nature. In your relationship with Allah, do you resent obstacles as hindrances, or do you accept them as His wisdom to shape your character? How would your patience change if you saw each obstacle as a necessary determiner of your growth?
?
If the external obstacle (the medium) is essential for motion, what does that mean for your reliance on Allah? Do you trust that He places the right obstacles in your path? Are you willing to examine the 'consistency' of your daily environment and adjust it to better support your journey towards Him?

Impossibility of Internal Obstacle in Natural Motion of Simple Bodies
Refutes the possibility of internal obstacle in natural motion of simple bodies and allows it in composites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an internal obstacle is inconceivable in natural motion of simple bodies and how this differs from composite bodies.

Core Idea
In simple bodies, nature itself would be the only internal obstacle, which is contradictory.

The commentator argues that in simple bodies, an internal obstacle cannot exist because any internal factor would be the nature itself, which is the cause of motion. However, in composite bodies, internal obstruction from material parts is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the claim that internal obstacle is inconceivable in natural motion.
2
Understand the refutation
Note the objection about a bird's soul and see how the commentator responds by clarifying that internal obstacle is more general, but in simples nature is the only candidate.
3
Recognize the exception for composites
Understand that in composite bodies, internal obstruction from material parts is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( والقابل للحركة إلخ هذا زائد على كلام البعض يعني أن الجسم من حيث إنه جسم قابل للحركة مطلقاً وليس فيه تحديد لمرتبة من مراتبها وإلا لكانت تلك لازمة للجسمية في جميع الأحوال غير قابلة لمرتبة أخرى بل التفاوت إنما يكون فيه بحسب المعاوق الداخلي أو الخارجي وقد أورد على هذا مثل ما أورد على القاسر بأنه إذا لم يكن تفاوت بسببه كان ذلك الزمان محفوظاً في الأحوال الثلاث فلا يتم الاستدلال وأنت خبير بعدم وروده على ما حررناه . قوله: (إذ لو لم يعاوقه إلخ) لأنه على تقدير عدم المعاوق، إما أن لا يكون له تعلق بالحركة أو يكون له تعلق بالإعانة وعلى التقديرين لا يكون محدداً أما على الأول فظاهر وأما على الثاني لا أن يكون أمر آخر غير القوام كالقوة الجاذبة للمغناطيس مثلاً محدداً بحسب اختلافها في القوة والضعف . قوله : ( ولا يتصور في الحركة الطبيعية معاوق داخلي إلخ) أي إذا كانت في الأجسام البسيطة لأنه لا يكون ذلك المعاوق حينئذ إلا الطبيعة فاندفع ما قيل : ما ذكره من قوله : لاستحالة إنما يدل على كون الطبيعة معاوقاً والمعاوق الداخلي أعم منها فيجوز أن يكون نفساً كالطير الساقط من عدم مكان هو يطير إليه . قوله : ( فالخارج هو قوام ما في المسافة قيل : لا نسلم ذلك لم لا يجوز أن يكون أمراً آخر غير القوام كالقوة الجاذبة للمغناطيس مثلاً فإنا لو أخذنا بيدنا قطعة من المغناطيس مع الحديد ثم أرسلنا الحديد فإنه يتحرك فيها المعاوقة الداخلية من أجزاء مادية والسر فيه أن حركة
His saying: 'And the recipient of motion, etc.' This is additional to the statement of that some (scholar), meaning that the body insofar as it is a body is receptive to motion absolutely and there is in it no determination of a rank of its ranks; otherwise that would be necessary for corporeality in all states, not susceptible to another rank. Rather variation occurs in it only according to internal or external obstacle. And an objection was raised against this like that raised against the compeller: if there is no variation due to it, that time is preserved in the three states, so the inference is not complete. But you are aware that it does not apply to what we have refined. His saying: 'For if it did not obstruct him, etc.' Because on the assumption of no obstacle, either it has no connection to motion or it has connection by assistance; in both cases it is not a determiner. As for the first, it is obvious. As for the second, because if the requirement of nature and the compeller is the utmost ranks of speed, assistance in it is inconceivable. And as for what was said: 'The other thing does not have to be an obstacle; rather we say that thing is inclination (mayl),' as that some (scholar) stated, it is refuted because that obstacle thing's determination of a limit of speed and slowness is by its determining a rank of the ranks of inclination; for nature or the compeller do not specify a rank of ranks of inclination; rather it is specified by the difference of the body having nature in quantity (smallness and largeness) and quality (rarefaction and condensation) or position (integration of parts and their expansion) or according to the fineness of what is in it of motion and its coarseness. And by what we mentioned, the contradiction between the two statements of that some (scholar) is refuted, where he said: 'The determiner of speed and slowness is the obstacle,' and he stated before this explanation that it is inclination. His saying: 'So the external' is the consistency, etc., because besides distance, mover, and moved, external things do not necessitate motion, so they cannot be a determiner for what accompanies it of speed and slowness. Thus what was said is refuted: 'Why not be something else besides consistency, like the magnetic attractive force, for example, a determiner according to its difference in strength and weakness?' His saying: 'And an internal obstacle is inconceivable in natural motion, etc.' i.e., when in simple bodies, because then that obstacle is only nature. Thus what was said is refuted: 'What he mentioned from his saying: 'due to impossibility' only indicates that nature is an obstacle, and the internal obstacle is more general than it, so it may be a soul, like a bird falling from a place it is flying to.' His saying: 'So the external is the consistency of what is in the distance.' It was said: 'We do not accept that; why not allow something else besides consistency, like the magnetic attractive force? For if we take in our hand a piece of magnet with iron and then release the iron, it moves naturally downwards and the magnet's force opposes it in motion and it accelerates according to its distance from the magnet.' His saying: 'And an internal obstacle is inconceivable in natural motion.' This is in the motions of simples, but in the motions of natural composites, internal obstruction from material parts is possible. The secret is that motion...
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on motion. It begins by explaining that the body itself, as a recipient of motion, has no inherent determination of speed; variation comes only from an obstacle, either internal or external. An objection is raised similar to one against the compeller: if the body itself causes no variation, time would be the same in all states. The commentator dismisses it. He then argues that without an obstacle, the obstacle has either no relation to motion or only assists; in neither case can it determine speed. A further objection suggests that the determining factor might be inclination (mayl), not an obstacle. The response is that the obstacle determines speed by determining a rank of inclination, since nature or compeller do not specify a rank; rather, it is specified by differences in the body (size, density, position, etc.). The commentator reconciles two statements of a previous scholar: one saying the obstacle is the determiner, another saying it is inclination. Next, he argues that the external obstacle is the consistency of the medium in the distance, and he refutes the possibility of another external factor like magnetic force, because only the medium necessarily accompanies motion. Regarding internal obstacles, he states they are inconceivable in simple natural bodies because nature itself would be the obstacle; an objection that a soul (like a bird) could be an internal obstacle is refuted by noting that this applies only to composite bodies, not simples. The passage ends with a mention that internal obstruction is possible in composites, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms

البسائط
المركبات
الطبيعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An internal obstacle can exist in any natural motion, such as a bird's soul opposing its fall.
Correction: Internal obstacle is impossible in simple bodies because nature itself would be the only internal factor; in composites, it is possible due to material parts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which type of motion is an internal obstacle possible?
A) Natural motion of simple bodies
B) Natural motion of composite bodies
C) Both simple and composite
D) Neither simple nor composite

Key Takeaway
Internal obstacle is impossible in simple natural bodies; it only occurs in composites.

?
The lesson shows that internal obstacles arise from composite, conflicting parts. Examine your life: which 'material parts'—worldly concerns, sins, distractions—are causing internal conflict and slowing your spiritual movement towards Allah? What would it mean to become 'simple' in your intention, with no counter-force within?
?
