Natural Repulsion and the Stone Under the Hand
Refutes a claim about natural repulsion and answers an objection about a stone under the hand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that natural repulsion can exist when an object is in its natural place, and how the objection about a stone under the hand is answered through the concept of center of gravity.

Core Idea
Natural repulsion only occurs when an object is not in its natural place.

This passage presents a key objection in the discussion of natural inclination (mayl tabi‘i). The author argues that repulsion only happens within the act itself, not in its beginning. The objector gives the example of a stone seeming to push downward even when on the ground. The answer involves the concept of center of gravity, showing that the stone is not truly in its natural place unless its center of gravity aligns with the world's center.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: a claim, its refutation, an objection, and an answer.
Pay attention to the key terms like مدافعة and مركز الثقل.
2
Identify the claim and its refutation
The passage begins with a claim: 'what is naturally sought is naturally fled from'. The author declares it false. Understand why the inference is only valid within the act of repulsion itself.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector uses the example of a stone under the hand to argue that repulsion exists even when the stone is on the ground. Consider the objector's reasoning: the stone's state is the same with or without the hand, so repulsion must be present.
4
Understand the answer
The author responds by distinguishing between being in a natural place and having the center of gravity aligned. The stone is not in its natural place until that alignment occurs. Then the explanation about parts clarifies when repulsion occurs.
Focus on the distinction between heavy bodies with actual parts and those without.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
Summarize the lesson: repulsion is not a property of being in the natural place; it is a motion toward it. The objection fails because it assumes the stone is already in its natural place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالمطلوب بالطبع مهروب عنه بالطبع وأنه باطل (وهذا الاستدلال (إنما يصح) ويتم (في نفس المدافعة) لأنها إما طلب لذلك المكان أو هرب عنه ) دون مبدئها ) فإنه إذا كان مبدأ المدافعة إلى ذلك المكان الطبيعي موجوداً بدون المدافعة لم يلزم طلب الحاصل وهو ظاهر لا يقال : إنا إذا وضعنا اليد تحت الحجر الموضوع على الأرض وجدنا منه مدافعة هابطة، ولا شك أن حاله إذا كان اليد تحته كحاله إذا لم تكن تحته فالمدافعة موجودة في الحجر حال حصوله في موضعه الطبيعي لأنا نقول : ليس ذلك الحجر في حيزه الطبيعي، وإنما يكون كذلك إذا كان مركز ثقله منطبقاً على مركز العالم وتوضيحه أنَّ الثقيل إذا كان ذا أجزاء موجودة بالفعل كان لكل واحد من أجزائه حظ من الثقل فكل واحد منها طالب لانطباق مركز ثقله على مركز العالم، ولا يكون هذا المطلوب حاصلا إلا لجزء من ذلك الثقيل فتكون المدافعة حاصلة في سائر أجزائه وإذا كان الثقيل ليس له أجزاء بالفعل فإذا انطبق مركز ثقله على مركز العالم لا يكون فيه مدافعة أصلاً لا في كله لأنه واجد للحالة المطلوبة له بالطبع ولا في أجزائه، إذ لیست موجودة بالفعل
That which is naturally sought is naturally fled from, and that is false. (And this inference is valid and complete only within the act of repulsion itself) because repulsion is either a seeking of that place or a flight from it, (not its beginning). For if the beginning of repulsion toward that natural place exists without repulsion, then the seeking of what is already attained does not follow—that is obvious. It cannot be said: when we put our hand under a stone placed on the ground, we find from it a downward repulsion, and there is no doubt that its state when the hand is under it is like its state when the hand is not under it; thus repulsion exists in the stone while it is in its natural place. For we say: that stone is not in its natural place; it is only so when its center of gravity coincides with the center of the world. The explanation is that if a heavy body actually has parts, each part has a share of weight, so each part seeks the coincidence of its center of gravity with the center of the world, and this sought thing is only attained for one part of that heavy body; thus repulsion occurs in its remaining parts. And if the heavy body has no actual parts, then when its center of gravity coincides with the center of the world, there is no repulsion at all—neither in its entirety because it possesses the state naturally sought, nor in its parts because they do not actually exist.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer within the discussion of natural inclination (mayl tabi‘i) and repulsion (madafe‘ah). The author first restates a claim: that what is naturally sought is naturally fled from, and declares it false. The inference about natural repulsion is said to be valid only within the act of repulsion itself (fī nafs al-mudāfa‘ah), not before it begins. If the beginning of the motion toward the natural place is present without repulsion, then the seeker cannot be said to seek what it already has. The objector then raises the case of a stone under the hand: when a hand is placed under a stone resting on the ground, the stone seems to push downward, implying it has repulsion even in its natural place. The answer explains that the stone is not truly in its natural place unless its center of gravity coincides with the center of the world. If the heavy body has actual parts, each part seeks that alignment, but only one part attains it, so repulsion occurs in the other parts. If it has no actual parts, there is no repulsion at all. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
مدافعة (madāfa‘ah)
ميل طبيعي (mayl ṭabī‘ī)
مركز الثقل (markaz al-thiql)
طلب بالطبع (ṭalab bi-l-ṭab‘)
هرب بالطبع (harab bi-l-ṭab‘)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A stone resting on the ground always has a natural repulsion or tendency to move downward.
Correction: The passage explains that repulsion only occurs when the center of gravity is not aligned with the world's center. If the stone is truly in its natural place (center of gravity aligned), there is no repulsion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the objection about the stone under the hand invalid?
A) Because the stone is not in its natural place unless its center of gravity coincides with the center of the world
B) Because repulsion only occurs in heavy bodies with actual parts
C) Because the hand itself causes the repulsion
D) Because the stone never actually has repulsion

Key Takeaway
Natural repulsion is not present when an object is in its true natural place (center of gravity aligned), but only when it is out of that place, as seen in the parts of a heavy body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a natural disposition (fitrah) to seek truth and goodness. In what areas of your life are you experiencing 'repulsion'—resistance, discomfort, or inner conflict—because you are out of alignment with that fitrah? What would it take for your center to align with what Allah wants for you?
?
The stone's parts seek alignment only when they are not yet aligned. Consider the 'parts' of your life—your habits, relationships, worship. Which parts are in disarray, causing inner turmoil? Will you let that discomfort push you to seek alignment, or will you remain stubbornly out of place?
?
If you were a heavy body with no actual parts, you would be perfectly at rest when at the center. But you are complex, with many parts pulling in different directions. Which direction pulls you most away from your spiritual center—your 'natural place' of submission to Allah? What one concrete step can you take today to bring one part closer to alignment?

Forced Inclination and Its Combination with Natural Inclination
Defines forced inclination, its first ruling, and the debate on combining with natural inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of forced inclination, its first ruling, and the two views on how it combines with natural inclination, including the refutation of one view.

Core Idea
Forced and natural inclinations can combine to produce stronger motion.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif tackles the concept of forced inclination (al-mayl al-qasrī) — the impetus imparted by an external force. The author explains that forced inclination can coexist with natural inclination in the same direction, and this gives rise to a philosophical discussion about the mechanism of their combination. We will explore the two main views on how the inclinations combine, and why one view is ultimately rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes a gloss and an objection at the end.
2
Identify the first ruling
The passage states that forced inclination has two rulings. Find the first ruling: that it may coexist with natural inclination toward one direction. The stone example illustrates this.
3
Understand the two views on combination
The passage presents two views: (1) nature and force each produce a degree of inclination, and together they yield a single, more intense inclination; (2) coexistence is only possible when there is resistance (e.g., air). Note how the author refutes the second view.
The refutation uses the variability of bodies (size, density).
4
Analyze the gloss and objection
The gloss clarifies that the combined inclination is not one of the three types (natural, forced, psychic). The objection about falling speed from different heights is answered by noting the accumulation of inclination.
This part adds nuance but is secondary to the main argument.
5
Synthesize the key takeaway
Summarize in your own words: forced inclination can coexist with natural inclination, and the correct view is that they produce one intensified inclination, not that resistance is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الميل القسري فأثبتوا له أيضاً حكمين: الأول قد يجامع الميل القسري الميل الطبيعي إلى جهة واحدة فإنَّ الحجر الذي يرمى إلى أسفل يكون أسرع نزولاً من الذي ينزل بنفسه مع تساويهما في الحجم والثقل . فقد اجتمع في الأول ميل طبيعي وميل غريب بسبب القاسر ، فلذلك كانت حركته أسرع . ويجوز أن يقال : إِنَّ الطبيعة وحدها تحدث مرتبة من مراتب الميل وكذلك القاسر فلما اجتمعا أحدثا مرتبة أشد مما يقتضيه كل واحد منهما على حده فلا يكون هناك إلا ميل واحد مستند إلى الطبيعة والقاسر معاً. وقال بعضهم إنما يجوز اجتماعهما إذا كان الجسم ممنواً بما يعاوقه كالحجر فإن الهواء يقاومه وبقدر تلك المقاومة يحصل الفتور فلا يبعد أن يحصل مع الميل الطبيعي ميل قسري وإذا لم يكن له معاوق كما إذا قدرنا المسافة خلاء كان اجتماعهما محالاً، لأنَّ الطبيعة إذا خلت عن العوائق أحدثت معلولها على أقصى ما يمكن فيكون الميل الطبيعي على ذلك التقدير بالغاً إلى نهاية الشدة فيستحيل أن يجامعه ميل غريب على أحد الوجهين، وهذا باطل بما اختلاف الجسم ذي الطبيعة في الصغر والكبر والتخلخل والتكاثف والاندماج والانتفاش فلا يرد أن الطبيعة نسبتها إلى جميع مراتب الميل على السوية فلا يقتضي مرتبة معينة كما مر بيانه . قوله : (إلا) ميل واحد مستند إلى الطبيعة والقاسر معاً في إشارة إلى أن ليس داخلاً في شيء من الأقسام الثلاثة لأنها أقسام لما يكون مستنداً إلى واحد منها . قوله : (ممنواً) في الصحاح منوته ومنيته إذا ابتليته. أعلى يكون أسرع من الذي من مكان أسفل مع تساويهما في الحجم والثقل حتى أن الأول ربما يصادم حيواناً فيقتله ولا كذلك النازل من أسفل ؟ قلت : سره اشتداد الميل في الأول وذلك لأن الطبيعة إذا لم تكن معوقة بالضد أوجدت الميل ولا يزال يزداد الميل ومعلوم أن تأثير الطبيعة وحدها أو مع ميل قليل ليس كتأثيرها مع الميول الكثيرة التي تقويها وتعضدها كما في المباحث المشرقية.
As for forced inclination (al-mayl al-qasrī), they also affirmed for it two rulings: First, forced inclination may coexist with natural inclination toward one direction. For the stone that is thrown downward descends faster than one that descends by itself, while they are equal in size and weight. So in the former, a natural inclination and a strange inclination due to the force coexist; therefore its motion is faster. And it is possible to say that nature alone produces a degree of inclination, and likewise the force; when they combine, they produce a degree more intense than each one alone requires, so there is only one inclination based on nature and force together. Some said that their coexistence is permissible only if the body is afflicted with something that opposes it, like the stone, for air resists it, and according to that resistance, slackening occurs; so it is not unlikely that together with natural inclination a forced inclination arises. But if it has no opposition, as if we suppose the distance to be a void, then their coexistence is impossible, because nature, free of obstacles, produces its effect to the utmost possible; so natural inclination in that estimation reaches the extreme intensity, and it is impossible for a strange inclination to coexist with it in either of the two ways. And this is false because of the difference of the body having nature in smallness and largeness, rarefaction and density, integration and expansion. So it cannot be said that nature's relation to all degrees of inclination is uniform, so it does not require a particular degree, as previously explained. His statement: '(except) one inclination based on nature and force together' indicates that it is not included in any of the three types, because they are types of what is based on one of them. His statement: '(mamnuwwan)' in the Sahah: 'manawtuhu and mayyattuhu' if you afflicted him. (Objection:) Is a stone falling from a higher place faster than one from a lower place, while they are equal in size and weight, so that the former might collide with an animal and kill it, but not the one descending from lower? I said: Its secret is the intensification of inclination in the former, because nature, when not opposed by a contrary, produces inclination, and inclination continues to increase. And it is known that the effect of nature alone or with a small inclination is not like its effect with many inclinations that strengthen and support it, as in the Eastern investigations.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defines forced inclination (al-mayl al-qasrī) and its two rulings. The first ruling is that forced inclination can coexist with natural inclination in the same direction, as in a stone thrown downward: it falls faster due to the combination. The author then presents two views on how the combination works. One view holds that nature and the force each produce a degree of inclination, and when combined they yield a single, more intense inclination. Another view restricts permissible coexistence to cases where the body faces resistance (like air); in a void, natural inclination would be maximal and cannot be increased. The author refutes this by noting that bodies vary in size and density, so nature's effect is not uniform. A gloss clarifies that the resultant single inclination is not one of the three types (natural, forced, psychic) because those are based on a single cause. Then an objection is raised: why is a stone falling from a higher place faster than one from a lower place? The answer is that nature, when unopposed, produces a growing inclination; the accumulated inclination strengthens the motion. This explanation draws from Eastern philosophical investigations.

Key Terms
ميل قسري (mayl qasrī)
ميل طبيعي (mayl ṭabī‘ī)
اجتماع الميلين (ijtimā‘ al-maylayn)
قاسر (qāsir)
ممنواً بما يعاوقه (mamnuwwan bi-mā yu‘āwiqhū)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think forced inclination can never exist alongside natural inclination, or that they always oppose each other.
Correction: The passage shows that forced and natural inclinations can coexist in the same direction, and that this combination results in stronger motion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first ruling of forced inclination?
A) It never coexists with natural inclination
B) It may coexist with natural inclination toward one direction
C) It always overpowers natural inclination
D) It only exists in a void

Key Takeaway
Forced inclination can coexist with natural inclination, and the correct view is that they produce a single intensified inclination when combined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If forced and natural inclination combine for greater effect, where in your life have you relied only on your 'natural' inclination (fitrah) without adding the 'force' of deliberate effort? What would change if you threw yourself into that action today?
?
The stone accumulates inclination the longer it falls. Are you accumulating good deeds and knowledge day by day, or are you descending by your own weight alone? What one 'throw' can you give yourself to increase your speed toward Allah this week?
?
The author refutes the idea that nature is uniform across bodies. How do you use your unique differences—smallness, largeness, density of heart—as excuses instead of recognizing that they affect your capacity? Will you accept your own nature and then add forced inclination through discipline, or remain content with your natural descent?

Causality and Created Things

Critique of Nature Producing a Specific Degree of Inclination
Criticizes the claim that nature alone produces a specific degree of inclination, using infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nature, being uniform, cannot directly produce a variable inclination, and how positing an intermediary leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Nature's uniformity prevents it from causing specific degrees of inclination without an intermediary.

This passage critiques a claim that nature alone can produce a specific degree of inclination. Drawing on al-Ṭūsī's commentary, the author argues that since nature does not admit intensity or weakness, its relation to all degrees is uniform; therefore, it cannot directly cause a particular degree. The introduction of an intermediary, inclination, itself variable, is necessary, but even then nature's relation to all degrees of inclination is uniform. If one posits an additional mediator, an infinite regress follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then its translation. Note the logical flow of the argument.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the problem
The problem: how can a uniform nature cause a specific degree of motion? Understand that nature alone cannot produce variability.
Recall that inclination mediates between nature and motion.
3
Follow the infinite regress argument
If another mediator is introduced, it leads to tasalsul (infinite regress). See how the author uses this to reject the claim.
4
Reflect on the alternative
The alternative is to allow different causes for origin and degrees, but that would also apply to motion, making the distinction arbitrary.

Source Text

قوله : ( ويجوز أن يقال : إن الطبيعة وحدها تحدث مرتبة من مراتب الميل) فيه بحث إذ قد سبق نقلاً من شرح الإشارات للطوسي أن توسط الميل بين الطبيعة والحركة لأجل أن الطبيعة شيء لا يقبل الشدة والضعف فنسبتها إلى جميع الحركات المختلفة بهما على سواء فاقتضت أولاً أمراً يشتد ويضعف بحسب اختلاف الجسم ذي الطبيعة وبحسب ما في الخارج وهو الميل. ولا شك أن الميل أيضاً يشتد ويضعف فنسبة الطبيعة إلى جميع مراتبها على السوية فلا يجوز تحدث الطبيعة وحدها مرتبة من مراتبها كما زعمه الشارح وإن توسط بينهما أمر آخر لزم التسلسل فإن جوز استناد أصل الميل إلى الطبيعة ومراتبها إلى أمور مختلفة فليجز مثله في الحركة وإلا فالفرق تحكم . أن قوله : ( فلا يكون هناك إلا ميل واحد مستند إلى الطبيعة والقاسر معاً فإن قلت : قد سبق أن الميل منحصر في الأقسام الثلاثة أعني الطبيعي والقسري والنفساني فهذا الميل حينئذ من أي تلك الأقسام؟ قلت : الظاهر أن المنحصر في تلك الأقسام هو الميل الذي سببه واحد على أن الذي يقتضيه وجه الحصر السابق دخوله في القسري إذ يصدق عليه أنه بسبب خارج عن المحل فإن المركب من الداخل والخارج خارج والامتياز في الوضع بين محل الميل والمجموع المركب
His statement: '(And it is possible to say that nature alone produces a degree of inclination)' is debatable, because it has already been transmitted from the Commentary on the Pointers (Sharḥ al-Ishārāt) of al-Ṭūsī that the mediation of inclination between nature and motion is because nature is something that does not accept intensity and weakness; thus its relation to all different motions in intensity and weakness is uniform; so it first necessitates something that intensifies and weakens according to the difference of the body having nature and according to what is external—that is inclination. And there is no doubt that inclination also intensifies and weakens, so nature's relation to all its degrees is uniform; therefore it is not permissible for nature alone to produce a degree of its degrees, as the commentator (al-Shāriḥ) claimed. And if another thing mediates between them, then an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. If one allows the ascription of the origin of inclination to nature and its degrees to different things, then let the same be allowed for motion; otherwise the distinction is arbitrary. (The statement:) 'So there is only one inclination based on nature and force together'—if you say: it has already passed that inclination is restricted to the three types, namely natural, forced, and psychic; then this inclination, which of those types is it? I say: it is apparent that what is restricted to those types is the inclination whose cause is one; as for what the previous restriction requires, it is that it enters into forced (al-qasrī), because it is true of it that it is due to something external to the subject, for what is composed of the internal and external is external, and the distinction in situation between the locus of inclination and the composite whole.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of the claim that nature alone can produce a specific degree of inclination. The author argues that since nature does not admit intensity or weakness, its relation to all degrees is uniform; thus it cannot directly cause a specific degree. The mediation of inclination itself is to account for variation, but nature's relation to all degrees of inclination is also uniform. If one posits an additional mediator, an infinite regress would follow. The argument then addresses a classification issue: if a single inclination arises from both nature and force, which of the three types (natural, forced, psychic) does it belong to? The answer is that the three types only cover inclinations with a single cause; a combined inclination falls under forced inclination because the cause is external (the composite of internal and external is external). The distinction depends on the locus of inclination versus the composite subject.

Key Terms
توسط الميل (tawassuṭ al-mayl)
شدة وضعف (shidda wa ḍu‘f)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nature directly determines the degree of inclination.
Correction: The passage shows that nature's uniformity prevents it from producing a specific degree; an intermediary (inclination) is needed for variability.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that nature alone produces a degree of inclination?
A) Nature is capable of producing all degrees directly
B) Nature's relation to all degrees is uniform and it does not admit intensity or weakness
C) Inclination cannot intensify or weaken
D) The claim leads to infinite regress only if another mediator is added

Key Takeaway
Nature's uniformity implies it cannot directly cause a specific degree of inclination; a variable intermediary is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the regularity of nature, do you unconsciously attribute outcomes to 'nature' or 'luck' rather than to the direct act of Allah? How does this argument's insistence on a variable intermediary call you to see every specific blessing as a direct divine gift?
?
The infinite regress argument reveals the need for a stopping point. In your spiritual life, where do you ultimately anchor your certainty? Are there areas where you rely on an endless chain of justifications instead of placing your trust in Allah?
?
The lesson shows that distinguishing between the origin and degrees of inclination is arbitrary. Are there habits or virtues you attribute partly to your own effort and partly to Allah? How can you surrender both the origin and every degree of your actions entirely to Him?

Classification of Combined Inclination
Resolves the classification of a combined natural-forced inclination as forced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single inclination arising from both nature and force is classified as forced inclination.

Core Idea
A combined inclination from internal and external causes is considered forced.

After discussing the impossibility of nature alone producing a degree, the author addresses a classification issue: if a single inclination results from both nature and an external force, which of the three types (natural, forced, psychic) does it belong to? The answer hinges on whether the cause is one or multiple. The author argues that the restriction to three types applies to inclinations with a single cause; a combined cause makes the inclination forced because the composite of internal and external is external.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the question posed and the answer given.
The answer involves a key principle: what is composed of internal and external is external.
2
Understand the classification problem
Recall the three types: natural, forced, psychic. The combined inclination doesn't neatly fit.
3
Analyze the solution
The author says the three types are only for inclinations with one cause. A combined cause makes it forced because the composite cause is considered external.
The locus of inclination vs. the composite whole is distinguished.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider why the composite cause is considered external: because the external factor is dominant in the combination, or because the distinction is situational.
The argument appeals to the principle of dominance.

Nature Alone Cannot Produce Specific Degrees
Al-Jurjani refutes the claim that nature alone can produce a specific degree of inclination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nature alone cannot produce a specific degree of inclination, based on the argument of infinite regress and the nature of inclination as an intermediate.

Core Idea
Nature does not accept intensity; inclination must mediate to avoid infinite regress.

Al-Jurjani addresses an objection that claims nature alone can produce a specific degree of inclination. Drawing on al-Tusi's commentary, he argues that since nature is uniform and does not vary in intensity, it cannot account for degrees. Instead, inclination serves as an intermediate that can intensify and weaken. Any attempt to introduce another intermediate leads to infinite regress. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims nature alone can produce a degree of inclination. Read the Arabic passage carefully.
Notice the phrase 'ويجوز أن يقال' introduces the objection.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation cites al-Tusi: nature does not accept intensity/weakness, so it cannot produce varying degrees. Inclination mediates and itself intensifies.
The key is that nature's relation to all degrees is equal.
3
Grasp the infinite regress argument
If nature alone could produce a degree, an intermediate would be needed, leading to infinite regress. The only consistent view is to attribute the basis to nature and degrees to other factors.
This argument is crucial for kalam's causal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويجوز أن يقال : إن الطبيعة وحدها تحدث مرتبة من مراتب الميل) فيه بحث إذ قد سبق نقلاً من شرح الإشارات للطوسي أن توسط الميل بين الطبيعة والحركة لأجل أن الطبيعة شيء لا يقبل الشدة والضعف فنسبتها إلى جميع الحركات المختلفة بهما على سواء فاقتضت أولاً أمراً يشتد ويضعف بحسب اختلاف الجسم ذي الطبيعة وبحسب ما في الخارج وهو الميل. ولا شك أن الميل أيضاً يشتد ويضعف فنسبة الطبيعة إلى جميع مراتبها على السوية فلا يجوز تحدث الطبيعة وحدها مرتبة من مراتبها كما زعمه الشارح وإن وسط بينهما أمر آخر لزم التسلسل فإن جوز استناد أصل الميل إلى الطبيعة ومراتبها إلى أمور مختلفة فليجز مثله في الحركة وإلا فالفرق تحكم . قوله : ( فلا يكون هناك إلا ميل واحد مستند إلى الطبيعة والقاسر معاً فإن قلت : قد سبق أن الميل منحصر في الأقسام الثلاثة أعني الطبيعي والقسري والنفساني فهذا الميل حينئذ من أي تلك الأقسام؟ قلت : الظاهر أن المنحصر في تلك الأقسام هو الميل الذي سببه واحد على أن الذي يقتضيه وجه الحصر السابق دخوله في القسري إذ يصدق عليه أنه بسبب خارج عن المحل فإن المركب من الداخل والخارج خارج والامتياز في الوضع بين محل الميل والمجموع المركب ذكرناه من أن الطبيعة وحدها جاز أن تقوى على مرتبة من مراتب الميل ولا تقوى على ما أشد منها، وكذلك القاسر وحده ربما يقوى على مرتبة دون أخرى فإذا اجتمعا أحدثا مرتبة أشد وأقوى أو أحدث كل واحد منهما أشد ما يقوى عليه من مراتبه . قوله : ( من أنَّ الطبيعة وحدها من غير اعتبار القاسر . قوله : ( جاز أن تقوى إلخ) باعتبار اختلاف الجسم ذي الطبيعة كما مر . قوله : ( القاسر وحده ) أي بدون الطبيعة . قوله : ( ربما يقوى إلخ ) باعتبار المعاوق الخارجي أو اختلاف الجسم المتحرك كما مر . أيضاً ثابت وإن ثبت خروجه بناء على إرادة الخروج بتمامه فهو داخل في الطبيعي إذ لم يذكر في هذا القسم إلا عدم خروج السبب وعدم المقارنة بالشعور، ولا شك أن الخروج المنفي في الطبيعي هو المثبت للقسري.
His statement: 'And it may be said that nature alone produces a degree of the degrees of inclination' — there is discussion about it, since it was previously transmitted from the commentary on al-Isharat by al-Tusi that the intermediacy of inclination between nature and motion is because nature is something that does not accept intensity and weakness, so its relation to all different motions is equal, so it first necessitated something that intensifies and weakens according to the difference of the body having a nature and according to what is in the external, which is inclination. And there is no doubt that inclination also intensifies and weakens, so nature's relation to all its degrees is equal, so it is not permissible for nature alone to produce a degree of its degrees, as the commentator claimed. And if another thing is intermediated between them, then infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. For if one allows the basis of inclination to be from nature and its degrees from different things, then let the same be allowed in motion, otherwise the distinction is arbitrary. His statement: 'So there is only one inclination based on both nature and the force together' — then if you say: it has preceded that inclination is confined to the three divisions, namely natural, forced, and psychic; so this inclination then is from which of those divisions? I say: it is apparent that what is confined to those divisions is the inclination whose cause is one, but what is required by the previous manner of division is its entry into forced (qasri) because it is true of it that it is due to a cause external to the locus, for the composite of internal and external is external, and the distinction in position between the locus of inclination and the composite. We mentioned that nature alone may be able to produce a degree of the degrees of inclination and not be able to what is more intense than it, and likewise the force alone may be able to produce a degree without another, so when they combine they produce a more intense and stronger degree, or each one produces the most intense it can of its degrees. His statement: 'that nature alone' — without consideration of the force. His statement: 'may be able to produce etc.' — considering the difference of the body having a nature, as passed. His statement: 'the force alone' — i.e., without nature. His statement: 'perhaps may be able to produce etc.' — considering the external obstacle or the difference of the moving body, as passed. Also established (aydan thabit); and if its exit is established based on intending complete exit, then it is included in natural, since in this section nothing is mentioned except the non-exit of the cause and the non-accompaniment by perception, and there is no doubt that the exit negated in natural is what affirms the forced.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage addresses two objections. First, it refutes the claim that nature alone can produce a specific degree of inclination. Citing al-Tusi, it explains that nature does not accept intensity or weakness, so its relation to all motions is equal; thus, inclination must mediate between nature and motion. If nature alone could produce a degree, an infinite regress would result. The second objection asks: when a single inclination arises from both nature and force, under which of the three types (natural, forced, psychic) does it fall? The answer: although the three types presuppose a single cause, this combined inclination is classified as forced (qasri) because its cause is partially external; the composite of internal and external is considered external. The commentary then clarifies that nature alone may produce a lower degree, and force alone may produce another, but when combined, they produce a stronger degree. The final note states that if the inclination's 'exit' (khuruj) is intended as complete, it would be natural, but the negated exit in natural is what affirms forced.

Key Terms
الطبيعة
الميل
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that nature alone cannot produce a specific degree of inclination?
A) Because nature is too weak.
B) Because nature does not accept intensity and weakness, so it would require an infinite regress.
C) Because inclination is always forced.
D) Because nature produces only one degree.

Key Takeaway
Nature alone cannot produce a specific degree of inclination because it does not vary in intensity; inclination must mediate, and any attempt to avoid this leads to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your 'nature'—your fixed habits or temperament—cannot produce specific degrees of faith or action on its own, what intermediary steps are you neglecting to cultivate? Are you relying on excuses rather than actively seeking means to intensify your devotion?
?
The argument shows that infinite regress is absurd. In your own spiritual life, do you make endless excuses or seek ever more reasons to delay change, avoiding the immediate intermediary (like sincere intention or repentance) that could break the cycle?
?
Nature alone is insufficient; an intermediary is necessary. What specific 'inclination'—a desire or intention—could you intentionally strengthen today through a concrete act, rather than passively waiting for change to happen on its own?

Classifying Combined Inclination: From Nature and Force
Al-Jurjani classifies the combined inclination from nature and force as forced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single inclination produced by both nature and force is classified, and why it falls under forced inclination (qasri).

Core Idea
A combined inclination is forced because the composite cause is external.

After establishing that nature alone cannot produce degrees, the passage addresses a further question: when a single inclination arises from both nature and force, which of the three known types (natural, forced, psychic) does it belong to? Al-Jurjani explains that the three types assume a single cause, but here the cause is composite. He argues that the composite of internal and external is considered external, so the combined inclination falls under forced inclination. He also discusses how nature alone and force alone each have limits, but together they produce a stronger degree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
The passage asks: when inclination is from both nature and force, which type does it belong to? Read the Arabic.
The question is introduced by 'فإن قلت'.
2
Understand the classification
The previous division assumed a single cause. The combined inclination has a composite cause. The composite of internal and external is external, so it is forced (qasri).
Note the reasoning: the whole is considered external due to the external component.
3
Consider the degrees
Nature alone can produce a lower degree, force alone another, but together they produce a stronger degree. This shows the combined effect.
This part clarifies that the combination yields a more intense inclination.

قوله : ( ويجوز أن يقال : إن الطبيعة وحدها تحدث مرتبة من مراتب الميل) فيه بحث إذ قد سبق نقلاً من شرح الإشارات للطوسي أن توسط الميل بين الطبيعة والحركة لأجل أن الطبيعة شيء لا يقبل الشدة والضعف فنسبتها إلى جميع الحركات المختلفة بهما على سواء فاقتضت أولاً أمراً يشتد ويضعف بحسب اختلاف الجسم ذي الطبيعة وبحسب ما في الخارج وهو الميل. ولا شك أن الميل أيضاً يشتد ويضعف فنسبة الطبيعة إلى جميع مراتبها على السوية فلا يجوز تحدث الطبيعة وحدها مرتبة من مراتبها كما زعمه الشارح وإن وسط بينهما أمر آخر لزم التسلسل فإن جوز استناد أصل الميل إلى الطبيعة ومراتبها إلى أمور مختلفة فليجز مثله في الحركة وإلا فالفرق تحكم . قوله : ( فلا يكون هناك إلا ميل واحد مستند إلى الطبيعة والقاسر معاً فإن قلت : قد سبق أن الميل منحصر في الأقسام الثلاثة أعني الطبيعي والقسري والنفساني فهذا الميل حينئذ من أي تلك الأقسام؟ قلت : الظاهر أن المنحصر في تلك الأقسام هو الميل الذي سببه واحد على أن الذي يقتضيه وجه الحصر السابق دخوله في القسري إذ يصدق عليه أنه بسبب خارج عن المحل فإن المركب من الداخل والخارج خارج والامتياز في الوضع بين محل الميل والمجموع المركب ذكرناه من أن الطبيعة وحدها جاز أن تقوى على مرتبة من مراتب الميل ولا تقوى على ما أشد منها، وكذلك القاسر وحده ربما يقوى على مرتبة دون أخرى فإذا اجتمعا أحدثا مرتبة أشد وأقوى أو أحدث كل واحد منهما أشد ما يقوى عليه من مراتبه . قوله : ( من أنَّ الطبيعة وحدها من غير اعتبار القاسر . قوله : ( جاز أن تقوى إلخ) باعتبار اختلاف الجسم ذي الطبيعة كما مر . قوله : ( القاسر وحده ) أي بدون الطبيعة . قوله : ( ربما يقوى إلخ ) باعتبار المعاوق الخارجي أو اختلاف الجسم المتحرك كما مر . أيضاً ثابت وإن ثبت خروجه بناء على إرادة الخروج بتمامه فهو داخل في الطبيعي إذ لم يذكر في هذا القسم إلا عدم خروج السبب وعدم المقارنة بالشعور، ولا شك أن الخروج المنفي في الطبيعي هو المثبت للقسري.
His statement: 'And it may be said that nature alone produces a degree of the degrees of inclination' — there is discussion about it, since it was previously transmitted from the commentary on al-Isharat by al-Tusi that the intermediacy of inclination between nature and motion is because nature is something that does not accept intensity and weakness, so its relation to all different motions is equal, so it first necessitated something that intensifies and weakens according to the difference of the body having a nature and according to what is in the external, which is inclination. And there is no doubt that inclination also intensifies and weakens, so nature's relation to all its degrees is equal, so it is not permissible for nature alone to produce a degree of its degrees, as the commentator claimed. And if another thing is intermediated between them, then infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. For if one allows the basis of inclination to be from nature and its degrees from different things, then let the same be allowed in motion, otherwise the distinction is arbitrary. His statement: 'So there is only one inclination based on both nature and the force together' — then if you say: it has preceded that inclination is confined to the three divisions, namely natural, forced, and psychic; so this inclination then is from which of those divisions? I say: it is apparent that what is confined to those divisions is the inclination whose cause is one, but what is required by the previous manner of division is its entry into forced (qasri) because it is true of it that it is due to a cause external to the locus, for the composite of internal and external is external, and the distinction in position between the locus of inclination and the composite. We mentioned that nature alone may be able to produce a degree of the degrees of inclination and not be able to what is more intense than it, and likewise the force alone may be able to produce a degree without another, so when they combine they produce a more intense and stronger degree, or each one produces the most intense it can of its degrees. His statement: 'that nature alone' — without consideration of the force. His statement: 'may be able to produce etc.' — considering the difference of the body having a nature, as passed. His statement: 'the force alone' — i.e., without nature. His statement: 'perhaps may be able to produce etc.' — considering the external obstacle or the difference of the moving body, as passed. Also established (aydan thabit); and if its exit is established based on intending complete exit, then it is included in natural, since in this section nothing is mentioned except the non-exit of the cause and the non-accompaniment by perception, and there is no doubt that the exit negated in natural is what affirms the forced.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage addresses two objections. First, it refutes the claim that nature alone can produce a specific degree of inclination. Citing al-Tusi, it explains that nature does not accept intensity or weakness, so its relation to all motions is equal; thus, inclination must mediate between nature and motion. If nature alone could produce a degree, an infinite regress would result. The second objection asks: when a single inclination arises from both nature and force, under which of the three types (natural, forced, psychic) does it fall? The answer: although the three types presuppose a single cause, this combined inclination is classified as forced (qasri) because its cause is partially external; the composite of internal and external is considered external. The commentary then clarifies that nature alone may produce a lower degree, and force alone may produce another, but when combined, they produce a stronger degree. The final note states that if the inclination's 'exit' (khuruj) is intended as complete, it would be natural, but the negated exit in natural is what affirms forced.

Key Terms

القاسر
القسري
الخروج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the combined inclination is a new type distinct from the three.
Correction: The passage clarifies that it falls under forced inclination due to the external cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which type does a single inclination caused by both nature and force fall?
A) Natural
B) Forced
C) Psychic
D) A new type

Key Takeaway
A single inclination caused by both nature and force is classified as forced (qasri) because the composite cause is considered external.

Inclination as Repulsion vs. Inclination as Cause
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between inclination as repulsion and as cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why natural and forced inclination cannot combine as repulsions towards two directions simultaneously, but their causes can coexist.

Core Idea
Repulsions cannot face both ways, but their causes can.

In this passage, al-Jurjani tackles a key question in kalam physics: can a body have both natural and forced inclination towards opposite directions at the same time? He clarifies that the answer depends on how we define 'inclination'—whether as the repulsion itself or as its underlying cause. Let's follow his precise distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two interpretations
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that al-Jurjani presents two possible meanings of 'inclination': as the repulsion (mudafa'a) itself, and as the cause (mabda') of that repulsion.
The distinction is crucial for the whole argument.
2
Understand the impossibility under the first interpretation
If inclination means repulsion, then two repulsions towards opposite directions cannot coexist in one body at the same time, because that would require the body to both push and withdraw from the same direction.
Consider the example of a stone thrown upward: it has upward repulsion, not downward.
3
See the possibility under the second interpretation
If inclination means the cause of repulsion, then the causes can coexist. Al-Jurjani illustrates with two stones thrown upward with the same force but different sizes: they differ in receptivity, showing that the causes (like weight) can be present simultaneously.
The example shows that causes can combine even if the repulsions themselves cannot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني أنهما ) أن الميل القسري والطبيعي هل يجتمعان إلى جهتين الحق أنه إنْ أريد بالميل المدافعة نفسها فلا يجتمع الميلان لامتناع المدافعة إلى جهتين في حالة واحدة بالضرورة إذ يستحيل أن يكون في شيء مدافعة إلى جهة وفيه مع ذلك التنحي عنها فليس في الحجر المرمي إلى فوق مدافعه هابطة وإن أريد بالميل مبدؤها فنعم إذ يجوز اجتماع مبدأ المدافعة إلى جهة مع مبدأ المدافعة إلى جهة أخرى بل يجوز اجتماع إحدى هاتين المدافعتين مع مبدأ الأخرى فإنَّ الحجرين المرميين إلى فوق بقوة واحدة إذا اختلفا في الصغر والكبر تفاوتا في قبولهما . قوله : ( لامتناع إلخ) قيل : قد مر سابقاً أنَّ في الحجر الموضوع على الأرض مدافعة هابطة فإذا جره أحد يجتمع المدافعة القسرية والطبيعية فيه والجواب : لا نسلم اجتماعهما معاً فيه، بل كل واحد منهما في زمان غير زمان الآخر لكن لقصر الزمانين المتعاقبين يتوهم اجتماعهما معاً فيه وإنما تلبد الأرض عند الانجرار لخشونة الحجر لا للمدافعة . قوله : ( وفيه مع ذلك إلخ) بمعنى أنَّ المدافعة إلى جهة أخرى يستلزم التنحي من الجهة الأولى فيلزم اجتماع المدافعة مع التنحي إلى جهة واحدة . أيضاً ثابت وإن ثبت خروجه بناء على إرادة الخروج بتمامه فهو داخل في الطبيعي إذ لم يذكر في هذا القسم إلا عدم خروج السبب وعدم المقارنة بالشعور ، ولا شك أن الخروج المنفي في الطبيعي هو المثبت للقسري. أيضاً غير مسلم كما ذكرنا في الحلقة المتجاذبة .
(The second) that whether natural and forced inclination combine towards two directions. The truth is that if by inclination is meant the repulsion (al-mudafa'a) itself, then the two inclinations do not combine due to the impossibility of repulsion towards two directions in one state necessarily, for it is impossible for something to have repulsion towards a direction and have withdrawal from it. So in the stone thrown upward there is no downward repulsion. And if by inclination is meant its cause (mabda'uha), then yes, for it is permissible for the cause of repulsion towards a direction to combine with the cause of repulsion towards another direction; rather, it is permissible for one of these two repulsions to combine with the cause of the other. For the two stones thrown upward with the same force, if they differ in smallness and largeness, they differ in their receptivity. His statement: 'due to impossibility etc.' It is said: it has passed earlier that in the stone placed on the ground there is a downward repulsion, so if someone drags it, forced and natural repulsion combine in it. The answer: we do not accept that they combine simultaneously in it; rather, each one is in a time other than the time of the other, but due to the shortness of the successive times, they are imagined to combine. And the ground only becomes packed due to the roughness of the stone, not due to repulsion. His statement: 'and in it, along with that' etc. means that repulsion towards another direction necessitates withdrawal from the first direction, so it necessitates the combination of repulsion and withdrawal towards one direction. Also established; and if its exit is established based on intending complete exit, then it is included in natural, since in this section nothing is mentioned except the non-exit of the cause and the non-accompaniment by perception, and there is no doubt that the exit negated in natural is what affirms the forced. Also not conceded (aydan ghayr musallam) as we mentioned in the case of the two pulled rings (al-halqa al-mutajadhiba).
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage discusses whether natural and forced inclination can combine towards two different directions. The author distinguishes between inclination as the repulsion (mudafa'a) itself and as its cause (mabda'). If taken as repulsion, it cannot be towards two directions simultaneously because a body cannot repel in one direction and withdraw from it at the same time. However, if taken as the cause of the repulsion, it is possible for the cause of repulsion in one direction to combine with the cause of repulsion in another. An objection is raised: a stone on the ground has downward natural repulsion, so if dragged, forced and natural repulsion seem to combine. The answer: they do not combine in the same moment; they occur in rapid succession, giving the illusion of simultaneity. The packing of the ground is due to friction, not repulsion. The commentary then adds that if the 'exit' (khuruj) is considered complete, it would be natural; and the final remark references the example of two mutually attracting rings, where such combination is also not conceded.

Key Terms
المدافعة
المبدأ
الجهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body cannot have any tendency towards two directions at all, whether as repulsion or cause.
Correction: This passage shows that while repulsions cannot be simultaneous, the underlying causes of those repulsions can coexist.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jurjani, under which interpretation can natural and forced inclination combine towards two directions?
A) When inclination means the repulsion itself.
B) When inclination means the cause of repulsion.
C) Under both interpretations.
D) Under neither interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Inclination as a physical repulsion cannot be directed to two opposite directions at once, but the underlying cause of such repulsion can coexist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel conflicted between two opposing desires, do you tend to focus on the immediate urges (the repulsions) or do you pause to examine the deeper causes behind them? How might this passage's distinction help you gain clarity and avoid self-contradiction in your choices?
?
The passage teaches that underlying causes can coexist even when their effects seem mutually exclusive. Reflect on a time when you judged yourself harshly for having two conflicting emotions—e.g., love for someone and frustration with them. Can you now see that both feelings might have different causes that can authentically coexist? Write down one specific cause for each emotion, and commit to addressing causes rather than suppressing feelings.
?
Al-Jurjani uses a physical example to illustrate a logical point. In your spiritual journey, do you ever mistake the superficial manifestation of a struggle (e.g., laziness in prayer) for its root cause (e.g., lack of certainty or attachment to worldly comfort)? What would it look like to treat the cause rather than the symptom this week?

Answering the Objection of the Dragged Stone
Al-Jurjani answers an objection about a dragged stone and further clarifies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the apparent conflict when a stone on the ground is dragged, appearing to have both natural downward repulsion and forced upward repulsion simultaneously.

Core Idea
Simultaneous repulsions are only an illusion of rapid succession.

Al-Jurjani now addresses a concrete objection: a stone placed on the ground naturally has a downward repulsion; when dragged, it seems to acquire an upward repulsion as well. How can this be? He responds by clarifying that the two repulsions do not occur at the same moment but alternate rapidly, and the ground's packing is due to friction, not repulsion. Let's examine his precise answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection as presented
The objector claims that a stone on the ground naturally has downward repulsion; when dragged, forced upward repulsion joins it, so both repulsions seem present at once.
This is a real-world example that tests the earlier distinction.
2
Understand the answer: not simultaneous but successive
Al-Jurjani denies that the two repulsions coexist in the same moment. Instead, they occur in rapid alternation, creating the illusion of simultaneity.
The key is that time is discrete in kalam atomism.
3
Note the explanation about ground packing
The ground becomes packed under the stone due to the stone's roughness (friction), not due to any repulsion. This distinguishes between physical effects and inclination.
This shows that not all apparent effects are caused by inclination.
4
Consider further remarks
Al-Jurjani also comments on how repulsion in one direction necessitates withdrawal from the opposite direction, and refers to the example of two mutually attracting rings where such combination is not conceded.
This reinforces the impossibility of simultaneous repulsions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

How does this realization call you to greater honesty and intentionality in your relationship with Allah?

Combining Natural and Coercive Inclinations
Distinguishes two senses of inclination and argues they can combine in principle but not as actual forces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that natural and coercive inclinations can combine in principle but not as actual moving forces, and why this distinction is crucial for causality in kalam.

Core Idea
Inclinations combine in principle, not in actuality.

This passage tackles a subtle question in causal theory: can two different inclinations—one natural, one coercive—simultaneously exist in a single body? The answer depends on what we mean by 'inclination.' The author distinguishes between the actual moving force and its underlying principle, and uses the example of stones thrown upward to illustrate how principles can combine without producing contradictory actual motions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument structure.
Notice the author's use of conditional reasoning and examples.
2
Identify the two senses of 'inclination'
The author distinguishes between inclination as actual moving force (mudafa'a) and as principle (mabda'). Understand why the former cannot combine but the latter can.
This is the core of the lesson.
3
Analyze the stone example
The example of two stones thrown upward shows that principles combine because the stones differ in speed due to nature. Trace how this supports the author's position.
Focus on why the difference in speed requires a natural principle.
4
Examine the objection and its rebuttal
The objection claims that combining principles would combine actual forces. See how the author answers by noting that the effect may be conditional.
This refines the causal framework.

ذكرناه من أن الطبيعة وحدها جاز أن تقوى على مرتبة من مراتب الميل ولا تقوى على ما أشد منها، وكذلك القاسر وحده ربما يقوى على مرتبة دون أخرى فإذا اجتمعا أحدثا مرتبة أشد وأقوى أو أحدث كل واحد منهما أشد ما يقوى عليه من مراتبه ( الثاني أنهما ) أن الميل القسري والطبيعي ) هل يجتمعان إلى جهتين الحق أنه إنْ أريد ) بالميل ( المدافعة نفسها فلا يجتمع الميلان ) لامتناع المدافعة إلى جهتين في حالة واحدة بالضرورة ) إذ يستحيل أن يكون في شيء مدافعة إلى جهة وفيه مع ذلك التنحي عنها فليس في الحجر المرمي إلى فوق مدافعه هابطة ( وإن أريد ) بالميل ( مبدؤها فنعم ) إذ يجوز اجتماع مبدأ المدافعة إلى جهة مع مبدأ المدافعة إلى جهة أخرى بل يجوز اجتماع إحدى هاتين المدافعتين مع مبدأ الأخرى (فإنَّ الحجرين المرميين) إلى فوق بقوة واحدة إذا اختلفا في الصغر والكبر تفاوتا في قبولهما للحركة ) فإنَّ الصغير أسرع حركة من الكبير ) وفيهما مبدأ المدافعة القسرية قطعاً) وذلك المبدأ قوة استفادها المتحرك من القاسر وتثبت فيه زماناً إلى أن يبطلها مصاكات مما يماسه ويتخرق ،به بل فيهما المدافعة القسرية بالفعل أيضا ( فلولا أن يكون فيهما ( مبدأ المدافعة الطبيعية لما تفاوتا في قبول الحركة فقد اجتمع مبدأ مدافعتين إلى جهتين بل اجتمع إحداهما مع مبدأ الأخرى وقد عرفت أن التفاوت بينهما مستند إلى الطبيعة فإن طبيعة الكبير أقوى وأشد معاوقة من طبيعة الصغير فليس يلزم أن يكون فيهما مبدأ المدافعة الطبيعية إلا أن يراد به نفس الطبيعة وما يقال من أن مبدأ المدافعة علة قريبة لها فلو اجتمع المبدآن لاجتمعت المدافعتان ممنوع لجواز أن يكون تأثير مبدأ المدافعة فيها مشروطاً بشرط يتخلف عنه ( وأما الميل النفساني فهو ) الميل (الإرادي وسأتيك في أبحاث الإرادة ما تعطفه ) وتضمه ( إليه ) أي إلى الميل النفساني فينكشف لك حاله زيادة انكشاف
We mentioned that nature alone can be strong enough for one rank of inclination but not for what is stronger, and likewise the coercer alone may be strong enough for one rank without another; when they combine they produce a stronger and more powerful rank, or each produces the strongest it can from its ranks. (Second: that the coercive and natural inclination) Do they combine towards two directions? The truth is that if by 'inclination' is meant the actual moving force itself, then the two inclinations do not combine, because moving towards two directions in one state is impossible by necessity, for it is impossible for a thing to have a moving force towards a direction and simultaneously have withdrawal from it; thus a stone thrown upward does not have a downward moving force. If by 'inclination' is meant its principle, then yes, for it is permissible for the principle of moving towards one direction to combine with the principle of moving towards another direction, indeed it is permissible for one of these two moving forces to combine with the principle of the other. For the two stones thrown upward with one force, if they differ in size, they differ in their acceptance of motion: the small one moves faster than the large one, and in both there is the principle of coercive moving force undoubtedly—that principle is a force acquired by the moving body from the coercer and remains in it for a time until frictions with what it contacts and penetrates nullify it. Indeed, in them there is the actual coercive moving force as well. Were it not that in them there is the principle of natural moving force, they would not differ in acceptance of motion; thus the principles of two moving forces towards two directions have combined, indeed one of them has combined with the principle of the other. You have known that the difference between them is due to nature, for the nature of the large is stronger and more resistant than the nature of the small; it is not necessary that in them be the principle of natural moving force unless by it is meant nature itself. And what is said—that the principle of moving force is a proximate cause of it, so if the two principles combined, the two moving forces would combine—is rejected, because it is possible that the effect of the principle of moving force in them is conditional upon a condition that may be absent. As for psychic inclination, it is voluntary inclination; I will bring you in the investigations of will what you may attach and append to it, i.e., to psychic inclination, so that its state becomes more fully disclosed to you.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a sustained argument about whether natural and coercive inclinations (al-mayl al-tabi'i wa al-qasri) can coexist in a single body. The author first recalls that nature alone can produce only a limited degree of inclination, and a coercer alone likewise; when both act together, they produce a stronger effect. He then poses the core question: can these two inclinations combine towards two different directions? He distinguishes two senses of 'inclination': the actual moving force (al-mudafa'a) itself, and its principle (mabda'). If by inclination we mean the actual moving force, then two such forces towards different directions cannot coexist, because a body cannot simultaneously be impelled towards one direction and be drawing away from it (as in a stone thrown upward, which has no downward moving force). If, however, we mean the principle of that force, then coexistence is possible. He supports this with the example of two stones thrown upward by the same coercive force: they differ in speed because of their differing natural weights, but both have the coercive principle. Their difference in motion shows that a natural principle must also be present, otherwise they would move identically. Thus, the principles of two different moving forces can coexist. He then refutes an objection: if the principle is a proximate cause of the moving force, then combining principles would entail combining forces. The author replies that the principle's effect may be conditional on a condition that is absent, so the forces need not both actualize. Finally, he mentions a third type, psychic inclination (al-mayl al-nafsani), which is voluntary, and promises further discussion in the section on will.

Key Terms
الميل
المدافعة
المبدأ
القسري
الطبيعي
النفساني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two inclinations combine, they must be actual moving forces.
Correction: The author explains that only the principles combine; the actual moving forces cannot combine because a body cannot have two opposite movements simultaneously.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can natural and coercive inclinations combine?
A) Only when they are both actual moving forces
B) Only when they are principles, not actual moving forces
C) They can never combine
D) Only when one is natural and the other is psychic

Key Takeaway
Natural and coercive inclinations can coexist as principles, but not as actual opposing movements, because actual movements cannot be in two directions at once.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, have you ever mistaken the presence of a desire (a principle) for a commitment to act (actual movement)? Can you name one area where you have a good inclination but have not actualized it? What condition is missing?
?
The stone thrown upward has no downward actual force, even though its nature pulls it down. Where in your heart do you claim to have two opposing actual intentions that cannot both be true? Are you willing to admit that only one is actual, and the other is merely a principle you entertain?
?
The author says the effect of a principle may depend on conditions. What conditions in your environment or inner state have prevented your good inclinations from becoming real actions? Are you taking responsibility to remove those conditions, or are you content with the principle alone?

Persistence of Necessary Inclinations
Affirms persistence of heaviness and lightness via sensory evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the persistence of necessary inclinations (heaviness and lightness) is affirmed through sensory observation, and how this parallels the persistence of sensible qualities like color and taste.

Core Idea
Heaviness and lightness persist in bodies, just as we see colors and tastes do.

In this lesson, we examine a concise but important argument from traditional Islamic theology: how do we know that inherent qualities like heaviness and lightness continue to exist in bodies? The author turns to direct sensory observation, drawing a parallel with colors and tastes. This grounding in empirical evidence is characteristic of the kalām approach to affirming persistent attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the subject and evidence
The passage first states the topic: the persistence of necessary inclinations (heaviness and lightness). Then it presents evidence: observation (mushāhada) judges it.
Notice that the evidence is empirical, not rational.
3
Analyze the analogy
The author strengthens the claim by analogizing to colors and tastes. Ask yourself: why does this analogy work? What do colors and tastes have in common with heaviness and lightness?
All are directly perceived by the senses.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument matters. It shows that physical attributes are stable and not transient, which has implications for causality and the nature of bodies in kalām.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It first states the topic: the persistence of necessary inclinations (al-iʿtimādāt al-lāzima), specifically heaviness and lightness, in heavy and light bodies. It then argues that empirical observation (al-mushāhada) confirms this persistence. To strengthen the claim, it draws an analogy with colors and tastes: just as sensory experience testifies that colors and tastes persist over time, so too does it testify that heaviness and lightness persist. Thus, the passage grounds the persistence of these qualities in direct sensory evidence, a common method in kalām.

The persistence of heaviness and lightness in bodies is known through direct sensory observation, just like the persistence of colors and tastes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You trust your senses for colors and tastes every day. But when have you ignored the 'heaviness' or 'lightness' in your soul—like a recurring conviction or a persistent doubt—because it made you uncomfortable? What would it mean to treat that inner weight as a real, persistent truth that Allah placed there for you to notice?
?
The passage says sensation testifies to the persistence of these qualities. Think of one spiritual ‘sensation’ you’ve had repeatedly—like a feeling of gratitude or a pang of guilt. Have you acted as if it’s a passing illusion rather than a lasting sign? What would change if you trusted its persistence as much as you trust the taste of sweetness?
?
If heaviness and lightness are always present in bodies, what ‘heavy’ responsibility have you been carrying that you keep trying to lighten? What if that persistence is not a burden but a divine signal that you are meant to carry it faithfully? How will you respond today?

Al-Jubba'i's Causal Claim: Moisture and Dryness
Al-Jubba'i presents his thesis and empirical evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's claim that moisture causes heaviness and dryness causes lightness, and the empirical evidence he provides.

Core Idea
Al-Jubba'i argues that hidden moisture and dryness cause weight differences.

In this lesson, we examine the theory of al-Jubba'i, a leading Mu'tazilite theologian, who sought to explain heaviness and lightness by appealing to hidden qualities of moisture and dryness. He supported his view with concrete examples from melting gold and burning wood. But does his evidence hold up? Let's read his own words.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Jubba'i's claim
Read the passage carefully. Note that he asserts moisture is the cause (mūjib) of heaviness and dryness is the cause of lightness.
2
Understand the examples
Identify the two examples: gold melting (revealing moisture) and wood burning (becoming calcined and ashy). How do these support his claim?
He assumes the moisture/dryness observed after heating existed before.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: Does the appearance of moisture after melting prove it was present before? This is the key assumption that will be challenged.
Consider the temporal sequence: cause must precede effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنه قال الجبائي : موجب الثقل الرطوبة وموجب الخفة اليبوسة يعني أن الاعتمادين اللازمين الطبيعيين معللان بعلتين هما الرطوبة واليبوسة فإنا إذا عرضنا الجسم الثقيل على النار كالذهب مثلا ذاب وظهرت رطوبته التي كانت موجودة فيه قبل العرض وإذا عرضنا الجسم الخفيف عليها كالخشب مثلاً تكلس أي صار كلساً وهو في الأصل الصاروج المركب من النورة واختلاطها وترمد أي صار رماداً إذ النار تزيده يبساً إفنائها للرطوبة القليلة التي كانت فيه حافظة للتأليف فيتفتت ويترمد ومنعه أبو هاشم وقال : بل هما كيفيتان حقيقيتان غير معللتين والجواب : عما تمسك به الجبائي أن يقال : الرطوبة التي في الذهب الذائب واليبوسة التي في الكلس غير موجودتين فيهما قبل مماسة النار حتى يستند إليهما الثقل والخفة الموجودتان قبلها وإنما تحدث الرطوبة واليبوسة فيهما عندها بإحداث الله تعالى إياهما على سبيل جري العادة وهما أي الذهب وما منه الكلس قبل أي قبل مماسة النار سيان متساويان في اليبس مع تخالفهما في الثقل والخفة قبلها فلا يكونان مستندين إلى الرطوبة واليبوسة كما توهمه كيف وما ذكره غير مطرد في الأحجار المكلسة التي أوقد عليها النار مدة مديدة حتى تفرقت رطوبتها بالكلية فإنها ثقيلة بشهادة الحس ولا رطوبة فيها أصلاً اتفاقاً وأما أنْ يقال بأنَّ الأجزاء المائية الظاهرة في حال الذوبان موجودة في الذهب قبله مع صلابته جداً وكذا الأجزاء المائية موجودة في الأحجار الصلبة التي تجعل مياهاً سيالة بالحيل كما يفعله أصحاب الإكسير قبل إذابتها فخرج هذه الفاء جواب أما أي القول بوجود الأجزاء المائية في الذهب والأحجار الصلبة قبل ذوبانها خروج عن حيز العقل ورفع للأمان عن المحسوسات إذ يجوز حينئذ أن يكون بين أيدينا أنهار جارية ولا نحس بها ولذا قال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق : لا نسلم أنَّ المذاب بعد الإذابة بل رطب هو باق على يبوسته وليس إنكار الرطوبة مع الميعان بأبعد من دعوى الرطوبة في الأحجار المحسوسة يبوستها
And among them is that al-Jubba'i said: The cause of heaviness is moisture and the cause of lightness is dryness, meaning that the two natural necessary inclinations are caused by two causes, namely moisture and dryness. For if we expose the heavy body to fire, like gold for example, it melts and its moisture appears, which existed in it before exposure; and if we expose the light body to it, like wood for example, it becomes calcined, i.e., becomes lime, which is originally the mortar composed of lime and its mixture, and becomes ashes, i.e., becomes ash, since fire increases it in dryness by destroying the little moisture that was in it preserving composition, so it crumbles and becomes ashes. And Abu Hashim objected and said: Rather, they are two real qualities, not caused. And the answer to what al-Jubba'i relied on is to say: The moisture in molten gold and the dryness in lime are not present in them before contact with fire, such that heaviness and lightness existing before it could be attributed to them; rather, moisture and dryness occur in them at that time by God's creation on the basis of habit. And they, i.e., gold and what lime is from, before contact with fire, are equal in dryness despite their difference in heaviness and lightness before it, so they are not attributed to moisture and dryness as he imagined. Indeed, what he mentioned is not consistent in the case of calcined stones that have been subjected to fire for a long time until their moisture has completely disappeared, for they are heavy by sensory testimony and have no moisture at all by agreement. As for the claim that the watery parts appearing during melting exist in gold before it despite its extreme hardness, and likewise watery parts exist in hard stones that are made into flowing water by tricks, as alchemists do, before their melting, this claim is a departure from reason and a removal of trust from sensible things, for then it would be possible that flowing rivers are before us and we do not perceive them. Therefore, al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: We do not accept that the melted thing after melting is moist; rather, it remains dry, and denying moisture with fluidity is not more far-fetched than claiming moisture in stones perceived to be dry.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubba'i's causal claim, his evidence, Abu Hashim's objection, and a detailed refutation. It begins with al-Jubba'i's thesis: moisture (ruṭūba) is the cause of heaviness, dryness (yubūsa) is the cause of lightness. He argues by examples: heating gold reveals its inherent moisture as it melts; heating wood makes it brittle and ashy, proving dryness increases. Abu Hashim objects that heaviness and lightness are unanalysed qualities (kayfiyyāt ḥaqīqiyya) without further causes. The author then refutes al-Jubba'i: the moisture appearing in molten gold and the dryness in calcined wood are not present before heating; they are created by God at the moment of heating, so they cannot be the cause of pre-existing heaviness or lightness. Moreover, before heating, gold and wood are equally dry yet differ in weight, disproving the causal link. Further counterexamples: heavily burnt stones are dry yet still heavy, contradicting the claim. The refutation also dismisses the idea that hidden watery parts exist in hard substances as absurd, because it would undermine sensory reliability—if such hidden water were possible, we could doubt rivers before us. Abu Ishaq's statement reinforces that fluidity (may'ān) does not imply moisture; a substance can flow while remaining dry.

Key Terms
الجبائي (al-Jubba'i)
رطوبة (moisture)
يبوسة (dryness)
ثقل (heaviness)
خفة (lightness)
اعتماد لازم (necessary inclination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fire
B) Moisture
C) Dryness
D) Inclination

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i argues that moisture and dryness are the hidden causes of heaviness and lightness, citing the behavior of gold and wood under fire as evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read al-Jubba'i's argument that moisture and dryness are the hidden causes of weight. Think of a belief you hold about why things happen in your life—something you assume is true without evidence. What is it, and are you willing to question your assumption as closely as al-Jubba'i's assumption about moisture was questioned?
?
The passage shows how even a great scholar can mistake correlation for causation. In your own worship or daily decisions, where might you be treating a mere coincidence as a spiritual cause? Commit to identifying one such assumption this week and test it against the Qur'an and Sunnah.
?
Al-Jubba'i's proof relies on the idea that what appears (moisture after heating) was there all along. Consider a sin that keeps appearing in your life—have you assumed it is 'just the way you are' without examining its true cause? What hidden root might you be ignoring? Resolve to dig deeper with sincere tawba and reflection.

Refutation of Al-Jubba'i: Objection and Counterarguments
Objection and detailed refutation of al-Jubba'i's causal claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's objection to al-Jubba'i's causal theory and the detailed refutation that exposes its flaws.

Core Idea
Moisture/dryness after heating cannot cause pre-existing heaviness/lightness.

In this lesson, we see the kalam response to al-Jubba'i. Abu Hashim, a key student, rejects the idea that heaviness and lightness need further causes—they are simple real qualities. Then a thorough refutation dismantles al-Jubba'i's evidence, showing that the moisture/dryness he observes are not present before the effect, and that counterexamples like calcined stones disprove the link.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Hashim's objection
He asserts that heaviness and lightness are unanalysed real qualities (kayfiyyāt ḥaqīqiyya), not caused by anything else.
2
Examine the main refutation
The answer argues that the moisture in molten gold and dryness in lime are not present before contact with fire; they are created by God at that moment, so they cannot be causes of pre-existing heaviness/lightness.
Also note the point that before heating, gold and wood are equally dry yet differ in weight.
3
Consider the counterexample
Calcined stones (burnt until all moisture is gone) are still heavy, directly disproving the claim that dryness causes lightness.
Sensory testimony confirms their heaviness, and there is no moisture by agreement.
4
Understand the absurdity argument
Claiming hidden watery parts in hard stones is absurd because it would undermine trust in the senses—rivers could be present but imperceptible.
5
Reflect on Abu Ishaq's remark
He asserts that melted substances are not necessarily moist; they remain dry though fluid, making the claim of hidden moisture even more far-fetched.
