ومنها أنه قال الجبائي : موجب الثقل الرطوبة وموجب الخفة اليبوسة يعني أن الاعتمادين اللازمين الطبيعيين معللان بعلتين هما الرطوبة واليبوسة فإنا إذا عرضنا الجسم الثقيل على النار كالذهب مثلا ذاب وظهرت رطوبته التي كانت موجودة فيه قبل العرض وإذا عرضنا الجسم الخفيف عليها كالخشب مثلاً تكلس أي صار كلساً وهو في الأصل الصاروج المركب من النورة واختلاطها وترمد أي صار رماداً إذ النار تزيده يبساً إفنائها للرطوبة القليلة التي كانت فيه حافظة للتأليف فيتفتت ويترمد ومنعه أبو هاشم وقال : بل هما كيفيتان حقيقيتان غير معللتين والجواب : عما تمسك به الجبائي أن يقال : الرطوبة التي في الذهب الذائب واليبوسة التي في الكلس غير موجودتين فيهما قبل مماسة النار حتى يستند إليهما الثقل والخفة الموجودتان قبلها وإنما تحدث الرطوبة واليبوسة فيهما عندها بإحداث الله تعالى إياهما على سبيل جري العادة وهما أي الذهب وما منه الكلس قبل أي قبل مماسة النار سيان متساويان في اليبس مع تخالفهما في الثقل والخفة قبلها فلا يكونان مستندين إلى الرطوبة واليبوسة كما توهمه كيف وما ذكره غير مطرد في الأحجار المكلسة التي أوقد عليها النار مدة مديدة حتى تفرقت رطوبتها بالكلية فإنها ثقيلة بشهادة الحس ولا رطوبة فيها أصلاً اتفاقاً وأما أنْ يقال بأنَّ الأجزاء المائية الظاهرة في حال الذوبان موجودة في الذهب قبله مع صلابته جداً وكذا الأجزاء المائية موجودة في الأحجار الصلبة التي تجعل مياهاً سيالة بالحيل كما يفعله أصحاب الإكسير قبل إذابتها فخرج هذه الفاء جواب أما أي القول بوجود الأجزاء المائية في الذهب والأحجار الصلبة قبل ذوبانها خروج عن حيز العقل ورفع للأمان عن المحسوسات إذ يجوز حينئذ أن يكون بين أيدينا أنهار جارية ولا نحس بها ولذا قال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق : لا نسلم أنَّ المذاب بعد الإذابة بل رطب هو باق على يبوسته وليس إنكار الرطوبة مع الميعان بأبعد من دعوى الرطوبة في الأحجار المحسوسة يبوستها
And among them is that al-Jubba'i said: The cause of heaviness is moisture and the cause of lightness is dryness, meaning that the two natural necessary inclinations are caused by two causes, namely moisture and dryness. For if we expose the heavy body to fire, like gold for example, it melts and its moisture appears, which existed in it before exposure; and if we expose the light body to it, like wood for example, it becomes calcined, i.e., becomes lime, which is originally the mortar composed of lime and its mixture, and becomes ashes, i.e., becomes ash, since fire increases it in dryness by destroying the little moisture that was in it preserving composition, so it crumbles and becomes ashes. And Abu Hashim objected and said: Rather, they are two real qualities, not caused. And the answer to what al-Jubba'i relied on is to say: The moisture in molten gold and the dryness in lime are not present in them before contact with fire, such that heaviness and lightness existing before it could be attributed to them; rather, moisture and dryness occur in them at that time by God's creation on the basis of habit. And they, i.e., gold and what lime is from, before contact with fire, are equal in dryness despite their difference in heaviness and lightness before it, so they are not attributed to moisture and dryness as he imagined. Indeed, what he mentioned is not consistent in the case of calcined stones that have been subjected to fire for a long time until their moisture has completely disappeared, for they are heavy by sensory testimony and have no moisture at all by agreement. As for the claim that the watery parts appearing during melting exist in gold before it despite its extreme hardness, and likewise watery parts exist in hard stones that are made into flowing water by tricks, as alchemists do, before their melting, this claim is a departure from reason and a removal of trust from sensible things, for then it would be possible that flowing rivers are before us and we do not perceive them. Therefore, al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: We do not accept that the melted thing after melting is moist; rather, it remains dry, and denying moisture with fluidity is not more far-fetched than claiming moisture in stones perceived to be dry.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubba'i's causal claim, his evidence, Abu Hashim's objection, and a detailed refutation. It begins with al-Jubba'i's thesis: moisture (ruṭūba) is the cause of heaviness, dryness (yubūsa) is the cause of lightness. He argues by examples: heating gold reveals its inherent moisture as it melts; heating wood makes it brittle and ashy, proving dryness increases. Abu Hashim objects that heaviness and lightness are unanalysed qualities (kayfiyyāt ḥaqīqiyya) without further causes. The author then refutes al-Jubba'i: the moisture appearing in molten gold and the dryness in calcined wood are not present before heating; they are created by God at the moment of heating, so they cannot be the cause of pre-existing heaviness or lightness. Moreover, before heating, gold and wood are equally dry yet differ in weight, disproving the causal link. Further counterexamples: heavily burnt stones are dry yet still heavy, contradicting the claim. The refutation also dismisses the idea that hidden watery parts exist in hard substances as absurd, because it would undermine sensory reliability—if such hidden water were possible, we could doubt rivers before us. Abu Ishaq's statement reinforces that fluidity (may'ān) does not imply moisture; a substance can flow while remaining dry.

Key Terms
أبو هاشم (Abu Hashim)
كيفية حقيقية (real quality)
جري العادة (habitual course)
أبو إسحاق (Abu Ishaq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Observing moisture after heating proves it was the cause of heaviness before heating.
Correction: The moisture/dryness are created only at the moment of heating; they cannot cause pre-existing heaviness/lightness. Counterexamples like calcined stones show heaviness without moisture.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does the refutation use to disprove al-Jubba'i's claim?
A) Melting ice
B) Calcined stones
C) Burning wood
D) Boiling water

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that the moisture/dryness observed after heating are not pre-existent, so they cannot be the causes of heaviness and lightness; counterexamples and the absurdity of hidden water undermine the theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a natural event, do you instinctively attribute it to physical causes rather than directly to Allah's creation? How does this lesson challenge your habitual thinking?
?
The refutation dismisses hidden watery parts as absurd because it would undermine trust in the senses. Are there 'hidden causes' you assume in your daily life that subtly weaken your reliance on Allah's direct power? Give an example.
?
If heaviness and lightness are created by Allah without a physical cause, are you willing to see every moment and every property of objects as a direct act of God? What would change in your dhikr or gratitude if you internalized this?

Refutation of al-Jubba'i on Heaviness and Moisture
Refutes al-Jubba'i's claim that heaviness is caused by moisture and dryness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments against attributing heaviness and lightness to moisture and dryness, and see how the Ash'ari perspective relies on habitual causation and empirical evidence.

Core Idea
Heaviness is not caused by moisture or dryness; God creates properties directly.

This passage is a direct refutation of al-Jubba'i, who claimed that heaviness and lightness result from the presence or absence of moisture. The responder uses both empirical examples and logical reasoning to show that moisture and dryness are not intrinsic causes but are created by God according to habit. We will examine the arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm and argument structure come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of parentheses to separate the structure of the response.
2
Identify the first argument: Gold and lime before fire
The responder argues that moisture and dryness are not present in gold and lime before they are heated. Therefore, the heaviness and lightness that exist before heating cannot be attributed to them.
3
Examine the second argument: Calcined stones
The responder points out that calcined stones (heated for a long time) have no moisture but are still heavy by sensory testimony. This directly contradicts al-Jubba'i's claim that heaviness requires moisture.
This is an empirical counterexample.
4
Understand the absurdity of the watery parts claim
The responder refutes the counterargument that water particles exist in solid substances like gold and hard stones before melting, calling it a departure from reason because it would undermine our trust in sensory evidence (e.g., invisible rivers).
The responder quotes Abu Ishaq to support that melting does not imply moisture.
5
Reflect on the principle of habitual causation
Throughout, the responder emphasizes that moisture and dryness are created by God at the time of heating 'according to the course of habit.' This reinforces the Ash'ari view that causal relations are not necessary but habitual.

والجواب: ( عما تمسك به الجبائي ( أن يقال : الرطوبة التي في الذهب الذائب واليبوسة التي في الكلس غير موجودتين فيهما قبل مماسة النار حتى يستند إليهما الثقل والخفة الموجودتان قبلها ( وإنما تحدث الرطوبة واليبوسة ( فيهما عندها بإحداث الله تعالى إياهما على سبيل جري العادة ( وهما ) أي الذهب وما منه الكلس ( قبل ) أي قبل مماسة النار ( سيان ) متساويان ( في اليبس ) مع تخالفهما في الثقل والخفة قبلها فلا يكونان مستندين إلى الرطوبة واليبوسة كما توهمه كيف وما ذكره غير مطرد في الأحجار المكلسة التي أوقد عليها النار مدة مديدة حتى تفرقت رطوبتها ) بالكلية فإنها ثقيلة بشهادة الحس ولا رطوبة فيها أصلاً اتفاقاً ( وأما أنْ يقال بأنَّ الأجزاء المائية ) الظاهرة في حال الذوبان ( موجودة في الذهب ) قبله ( مع صلابته ) جداً ( وكذا ) الأجزاء المائية موجودة (في الأحجار الصلبة ( التي تجعل مياهاً سيالة بالحيل كما يفعله أصحاب الإكسير قبل إذابتها فخرج هذه الفاء جواب أما أي القول بوجود الأجزاء المائية في الذهب والأحجار الصلبة قبل ذوبانها خروج ( عن حيز العقل) ورفع للأمان عن المحسوسات إذ يجوز حينئذ أن يكون بين أيدينا أنهار جارية ولا نحس بها ولذا قال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق : لا نسلم أنَّ المذاب بعد الإذابة بل رطب هو باق على يبوسته وليس إنكار الرطوبة مع الميعان بأبعد من دعوى الرطوبة في الأحجار المحسوسة يبوستها
And the answer: (concerning what al-Jubba'i clung to ( that it is said: The moisture present in molten gold and the dryness present in lime are not existent in them before contact with fire, such that the heaviness and lightness existing before it could be attributed to them. (But moisture and dryness occur ( in them at that time by God's creation of them according to the course of habit. (And both of them) i.e., gold and what lime is made from (before) i.e., before contact with fire (are equal) similar (in dryness) despite their difference in heaviness and lightness before it, so they are not attributed to moisture and dryness as he imagined. How? And what he mentioned is not consistent in calcined stones that have been exposed to fire for a long time until their moisture has completely dissipated, for they are heavy by sensory testimony and have no moisture in them at all by agreement. (And as for saying that the watery parts) apparent in the state of melting (exist in gold) before it (despite its extreme hardness) (and likewise) the watery parts exist (in hard stones that are turned into flowing water by artifice as the alchemists do before their melting, then this saying—the letter fa being the answer to 'amma—that is, the statement of the existence of watery parts in gold and hard stones before their melting is (a departure from reason) and a removal of trust from sensory things, for then it would be possible that there are flowing rivers in front of us and we do not perceive them. Therefore, al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: We do not accept that the molten thing after melting is moist; rather, it remains in its dryness, and denying moisture with fluidity is not more improbable than claiming moisture in stones whose dryness is perceived by the senses.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Jubba'i's claim that heaviness and lightness are caused by moisture and dryness. The responder argues: The moisture in molten gold and the dryness in lime did not exist before they were heated, so the heaviness and lightness present before heating cannot be attributed to them. Moisture and dryness are created by God at the moment of heating according to habit. Before heating, gold and lime are equal in dryness although they differ in weight, so their weight difference cannot be due to moisture or dryness. Additionally, calcined stones that have lost all moisture are still heavy, proving that heaviness does not depend on moisture. The responder then dismisses the counterclaim that water particles exist in hard substances before melting as irrational, as it would imply we might have undetectable rivers. Abu Ishaq supports this, denying that melting implies moisture.

Misconception: Moisture and dryness are intrinsic causes of weight.
Correction: Weight is not caused by moisture or dryness; these are accidents created by God according to habit.

Key Takeaway
Heaviness and lightness are not caused by moisture or dryness; they are directly created by Allah according to the habitual course of nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a belief about how the world works—something you thought was proven by observation—only to find that your own reasoning was clouded by assumptions you never questioned? What would it cost you to surrender that belief for the sake of trusting Allah's absolute power revealed in this lesson?
?
The passage says that claiming hidden moisture in dry stones 'would be possible that there are flowing rivers in front of us and we do not perceive them.' Are you living as if these are guaranteed by natural laws, or are you constantly aware that only Allah's will keeps you upright every instant?

Refuting Al-Jubba'i: The Real Cause of Floating and Sinking
A refutation of al-Jubba'i's explanation of floating and sinking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main arguments regarding the cause of floating and sinking, including al-Jubba'i's air-based theory, al-Amidi's objections, al-Iji's defense, and Abu Hashim's alternative based on real heaviness and lightness.

Core Idea
Floating is not caused by trapped air but by real lightness inherent in the body.

This lesson examines a key debate in Islamic kalam about the physical cause of floating and sinking. Al-Jubba'i proposed that floating results from air trapped in rarefied bodies, but al-Amidi raised powerful objections. The author al-Iji offers a nuanced reply, while Abu Hashim cuts to the root: floating and sinking are due to real, divinely-created attributes of heaviness and lightness.

Guided Path
1
Read Al-Jubba'i's Theory
Read the opening passage where al-Jubba'i explains that floating occurs because air enters rarefied parts and prevents sinking. Note his example of wood vs. iron.
Rarefaction allows air to cling; compactness prevents it.
2
Examine Al-Amidi's Objections
Al-Amidi presents two objections: gold sinks in mercury but silver floats (counterexample), and if air rises naturally, it should separate from the floating body.
Notice how he exposes inconsistency: air can't cling to gold yet it sinks.
3
Study the Author's Reply
Al-Iji replies that the composition or arrangement may create an inseparable state between air and other parts, so separation isn't necessary.
This defends the possibility of persistent clinging.
4
Learn Abu Hashim's Alternative
Finally, Abu Hashim simply states that floating and sinking are due to real heaviness and lightness, not air. Understand his dismissal of air's role.
This returns to the basic kalam principle of real accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أنه قال الجبائي : الجسم الذي يطفو على الماء كالخشب مثلا ( إنما يطفو ) عليه ( للهواء المتشبث به فإن أجزاء الخشب متخلخلة فيدخل الهواء فيما بينها ويتعلق بها ويمنعها من النزول فيه وإذا غمست صعدها الهواء الصاعد بخلاف الحديد فإنَّ أجزاءه مندمجة لم يتشبث بها الهواء فلذلك ترسب في الماء قال الآمدي : يلزم على الجبائي أن الذهب يرسب في الزئبق والفضة تطفو عليه مع أن أجزاءها غير متخلخلة حتى يتشبث بها الهواء ) ويلزمه ) أيضاً أنه يجب ( أن ينفصل عنه ) أي عن الجسم الطافي ( الهواء فيطفو) وحده ( وتبقى الأجزاء الأخرى راسبة ) في الماء لأنَّ الهواء عنده صاعد بطبعه والخشب راسب بطبعه فوجب أن ينفصل الآخر فيرسب الخشب ويطفو الهواء. قال المصنف : ( وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون التركيب ) الواقع بين الأجزاء الهوائية وغيرها في الجسم الطافي ( أو الوضع) الحاصل بين الهواء وأجزاء الطافي ( أفادهما ) أي أفاد الهواء والأجزاء الأخرى ( حالة موجبة للتلازم مانعة عن الانفصال ) يعني أن الجسم الطافي جاز أن يكون مركباً من أجزاء هوائية وغيرها تركيباً موجباً للتلازم بينهما بحيث يمنع عن انفصال الهواء عن سائر الأجزاء وجاز أيضاً أن يتخلخل الهواء فيما بين أجزائه على وضع مانع عن الانفصال فلا يلزم على شيء من هذين التقديرين أنه يجب انفصال الهواء ورسوب سائر الأجزاء ) وقال ابنه : ( أبو هاشم ( إنه للثقل والخفة ) أي الرسوب للثقل والطفو للخفة ( وهما ) أي الثقل والخفة ( أمران حقيقيان عارضان للجسم ) في نفسه ( كما مر) يقتضي أحدهما الرسوب والآخر الطفو ولا أثر للهواء في ذلك أصلاً .
And among them is that al-Jubba'i said: A body that floats on water like wood, for example, (floats) on it (due to the air clinging to it, for the parts of wood are rarefied, so air enters between them and clings to them and prevents them from sinking in it, and when immersed, the ascending air raises it, unlike iron, for its parts are compact, air does not cling to them, therefore it sinks in water. Al-Amidi said: It follows for al-Jubba'i that gold sinks in mercury and silver floats on it, although its parts are not rarefied so that air clings to them. And it also follows) that the air must (separate from it) i.e., from the floating body (and float) alone (and the other parts remain sinking) in water, because according to him air is ascending by nature and wood is sinking by nature, so the other must separate so that the wood sinks and the air floats. The author said: (There is consideration in this, for it is possible that the composition) occurring between the aerial parts and others in the floating body (or the position) obtained between the air and the parts of the floater (gave them) i.e., gave the air and the other parts (a state necessitating inseparability, preventing separation). Meaning that the floating body may be composed of aerial and other parts in a composition that necessitates inseparability, preventing the air from separating from the other parts, and it is also possible that the air is interposed among its parts in a position that prevents separation. So it does not follow on either of these two suppositions that the air must separate and the other parts sink. And his son Abu Hashim said: It is due to heaviness and lightness, i.e., sinking for heaviness and floating for lightness, and they i.e., heaviness and lightness are two real accidents inhering in the body itself, as previously mentioned, one necessitating sinking and the other floating, and air has no effect in that at all.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage contains al-Jubba'i's claim that floating is due to air trapped in rarefied parts, then al-Amidi's objection: gold sinks in mercury and silver floats, but their parts are not rarefied. Also, if air is naturally ascending and wood sinking, they should separate. The author (al-Iji) responds that the composition or arrangement may create an inseparable state, so separation is not necessary. Finally, Abu Hashim flatly states that floating and sinking are due to real heaviness and lightness, not air, as previously established.

Key Terms
الطفو
الرسوب
الهواء
التخلخل
التلازم
الثقل
الخفّة
الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Floating occurs because air is trapped in the body and pushes it up.
Correction: Floating is due to real lightness inhering in the body, not air; the air theory leads to absurdities.

Checkpoint
What is Abu Hashim's explanation of floating and sinking?
A) They are due to air clinging to the body
B) They are due to the rarefaction or compactness of parts
C) They are due to real heaviness and lightness inhering in the body
D) They are due to the position of the body relative to water

Key Takeaway
The real cause of floating is heaviness and lightness as real accidents in the body, not the presence or absence of air.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a piece of wood floating or a stone sinking, do you immediately think of physical laws, or do you remember that Allah directly creates these actions? How can you train yourself to see His hand in every event, no matter how ordinary?
?
This lesson rejects attributing causality to physical properties alone. Is there an area in your life where you rely on 'natural causes' rather than trusting Allah's direct control? What would change if you truly believed that He is the sole creator of every effect?
?
Al-Jubba'i's error was to see air as an independent cause. Do you ever explain away blessings or trials by secondary means, forgetting that Allah alone is the true giver and taker? How can you make the habit of thanking Him for the 'real causes' He creates?

Al-Āmidī's Objection and the Author's Response
Al-Āmidī objects to al-Jubbā'ī's theory of floating; author responds with composition/position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ījī defends al-Jubbā'ī's theory by suggesting that composition or position can prevent the separation of air from a floating body.

Core Idea
An objection is only necessary if no impediment exists.

Al-Āmidī raises two consequences he believes follow necessarily from al-Jubbā'ī's theory of floating. He argues that if air causes floating, then air should separate and float alone. The author responds by pointing out that composition or position could create a state that prevents separation. This lesson explores the objection and the defense, clarifying the role of impediments in physical causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read al-Āmidī's objection in the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two consequences he draws: gold/silver in mercury and the separation of air from wood.
Pay attention to the phrase 'يلزم على الجبائي'.
2
Identify the author's response
Find where the author says 'وفيه نظر' and read his two possibilities: composition and position. Understand how they prevent separation.
The response uses 'لجواز أن يكون التركيب... أو الوضع'.
3
Examine the commentary clarifications
The commentary explains each part of the author's response and addresses further objections. Read how it handles the example of silver floating and the concept of 'composition'.
Notice the distinction between compositional mixture and physical juxtaposition.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider how the objection is not automatically valid because necessary consequences can be blocked by impediments. Think about the broader principle in kalām: causality is not always necessary.
This connects to discussions about causal necessity in Islamic theology.

قال الآمدي : يلزم على الجبائي أن الذهب يرسب في الزئبق والفضة تطفو عليه مع أن أجزاءها غير متخلخلة حتى يتشبث بها الهواء ) ويلزمه ) أيضاً أنه يجب ( أن ينفصل عنه ) أي عن الجسم الطافي ( الهواء فيطفو) وحده ( وتبقى الأجزاء الأخرى راسبة ) في الماء لأنَّ الهواء عنده صاعد بطبعه والخشب راسب بطبعه فوجب أن ينفصل الآخر فيرسب الخشب ويطفو الهواء. قال المصنف : ( وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون التركيب ) الواقع بين الأجزاء الهوائية وغيرها في الجسم الطافي ( أو الوضع) الحاصل بين الهواء وأجزاء الطافي ( أفادهما ) أي أفاد الهواء والأجزاء الأخرى ( حالة موجبة للتلازم مانعة عن الانفصال ) يعني أن الجسم الطافي جاز أن يكون مركباً من أجزاء هوائية وغيرها تركيباً موجباً للتلازم بينهما بحيث يمنع عن انفصال الهواء عن سائر الأجزاء وجاز أيضاً أن يتخلخل الهواء فيما بين أجزائه على وضع مانع عن الانفصال فلا يلزم على شيء من هذين التقديرين أنه يجب انفصال الهواء ورسوب سائر الأجزاء . قوله: (إنَّ الذهب يرسب إلخ قد تحقق الرسوب والطفو من غير تخلخل الهواء فليجز أن يكون لطفو الخشبة على الماء سبب غير ذلك فلا يرد ما قيل أن الكلام في الطفو على الماء لا في الطفو المطلق . قوله : ( مركب من أجزاء (هوائية ليس المراد منه التركيب المزاجي بل التركيب الحاصل بين الأجزاء الهوائية المتخلخلة وبين أجزاء الخشب المجاورة لها فلا يرد أن حديث التركيب لا ورود له لأن الجبائي لم يقل بأن الأجزاء الهوائية التي صارت جزء الممتزج سبب الطفو . قوله: (بخلاف الحديد إلخ) قيل عليه : لم لا يرسب إذا جعل صفيحة؟ والجواب تحقق المانع عن وجود المقتضى وهو الاحتياج إلى زيادة خرق لا يطاوعه الماء. قوله : (قال الآمدي : يلزم على الجبائي إلخ إنما يرد إذا ثبت أن لا فرق بين طفو وطفو وإلا فكلام أبي علي في الطفو على الماء وقصة الزئبق لا تقريب لها حينئذ . قوله : ( لجواز أن يكون التركيب إلخ قيل : الكلام في الأجزاء الهوائية المجاورة للأجزاء الخشبية لا التي صارت جزء الممتزج كما في سائر المركبات على ما يراه الفلاسفة فحديث التركيب لا ورود له .
Al-Āmidī said: It follows necessarily for al-Jubbā'ī that gold sinks in mercury and silver floats on it, even though its parts are not rarefied such that air clings to them. And it also follows necessarily for him that the air must separate from the floating body, so it floats alone while the other parts remain sinking in water, because according to him air rises by nature and wood sinks by nature, so the other must separate, so the wood sinks and the air floats. The author said: (There is a consideration in this, because it is possible that the composition) occurring between the airy parts and others in the floating body (or the position) obtaining between the air and the parts of the floating body (cause them) i.e., cause the air and the other parts (a state necessitating conjunction, preventing separation). Meaning that the floating body could be composed of airy and other parts in a composition necessitating conjunction between them, such that it prevents the separation of air from the rest of the parts. It is also possible that the air is rarefied among its parts in a position preventing separation, so it does not follow from either of these two suppositions that separation of air and sinking of the rest of the parts is necessary. His saying: ('Indeed gold sinks, etc.') — sinking and floating have indeed been observed without rarefaction of air, so it is possible that there is another cause for the floating of wood on water, so what is said that the discussion is about floating on water not absolute floating does not refute this. His saying: ('Composed of airy parts') — what is intended is not compositional mixture but the composition between rarefied airy parts and adjacent parts of wood, so it is not refuted that the mention of composition has no relevance because al-Jubbā'ī did not say that the airy parts that became part of the mixture are the cause of floating. His saying: ('Unlike iron, etc.') — it is objected: why does it not sink when made into a thin plate? The answer: the impediment to the existence of the requirement is realized, namely the need for additional piercing that water does not yield to. His saying: ('Al-Āmidī said: It follows necessarily for al-Jubbā'ī, etc.') — it only refutes if it is established that there is no difference between one floating and another; otherwise, Abū 'Alī's statement is about floating on water and the case of mercury then has no relevance. His saying: ('Because it is possible that composition, etc.') — it is said: the discussion is about the airy parts adjacent to the wooden parts, not those that became part of the mixture as in other compounds according to the philosophers, so mentioning composition has no relevance.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by al-Āmidī against al-Jubbā'ī's theory that floating and sinking are due to the air's natural upward motion and the wood's natural downward motion, with air clinging to wood parts. Al-Āmidī raises two necessary consequences: first, gold sinks in mercury while silver floats on it despite silver's parts being compact without air clinging; second, if air is the cause of floating, then air should separate from the wood and float alone while the wood sinks, because air rises naturally and wood sinks naturally. The author (al-Ījī) responds by suggesting two possibilities that prevent separation: either the composition of airy and other parts in the floating body necessitates conjunction, or the positioning of air among the parts prevents separation. The commentator then clarifies each point: the example of silver floating shows floating without air rarefaction, so the cause could be something else; 'composition' here means physical juxtaposition, not mixture; and the objection about iron not sinking as a thin plate is answered by the need to overcome water's resistance. The commentary also refines the objection by stating that al-Āmidī's argument only holds if there is no distinction between floating on water and floating on mercury, and that the discussion of composition is relevant only if the airy parts are adjacent, not mixed.

الوضع
التلازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's response completely dismisses al-Āmidī's objection.
Correction: The author does not dismiss it but shows it is not necessary because there could be impediments preventing separation. The objection is valid only if no impediment exists.

Checkpoint
Why does the author (al-Ījī) say there is a 'nazar' (consideration) in al-Āmidī's objection?
A) Because al-Āmidī's argument is logically invalid.
B) Because it is possible that composition or position prevents the separation of air from the floating body.
C) Because gold and silver behave differently than wood.
D) Because al-Āmidī misunderstood al-Jubbā'ī's theory.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ījī's response shows that necessary consequences only follow if no impediment exists; thus al-Āmidī's objection is not conclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone's argument because you assumed a necessary consequence must follow, without pausing to consider whether there might be hidden impediments? Think of a specific recent disagreement. What would it look like for you to adopt al-Ījī's careful, possibility-seeking approach in that situation? Commit to one conversation this week where you will intentionally look for 'impediments' before concluding.
?
Your own faith journey may have moments where you felt a certain belief or practice was logically necessary, only to later discover that an impediment (like ignorance or spiritual blindness) was preventing you from seeing the truth. What is one area of your life right now where you are assuming a necessary consequence, and how can you actively seek out possible impediments (e.g., pride, fear, laziness) that might be clouding your understanding? Write down one concrete step to remove that impediment.
?
The author uses the possibility of composition and position to defend a theory. When you face an objection to your faith or worldview, do you immediately search for possible impediments that could explain why the objection doesn't hold? Or do you tend to either dismiss the objection outright or be swayed by it? This week, practice the author's method: take one objection you've heard, and sincerely ask yourself, 'What possible impediments (ignorance, context, hidden assumptions) could make this objection not necessary?' Share your finding with one person.

Abū Hāshim's Theory of Heaviness and Lightness and Its Critics
Abū Hāshim asserts heaviness and lightness are real causes; two objections are raised.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abū Hāshim's theory that heaviness and lightness are real attributes causing sinking and floating, and the two key objections raised against it.

Core Idea
Heaviness and lightness are real causes, but objections show they aren't absolute.

Abū Hāshim, a prominent Mu'tazilī theologian, proposed that sinking and floating are caused by two real attributes inherent in bodies: heaviness for sinking, lightness for floating. He denied any role for air. However, two empirical objections challenge this view: iron plates float despite equal weight, and a tiny iron grain sinks while huge wood floats. Let's examine the theory and these objections.

Guided Path
1
Read Abū Hāshim's claim
Read the passage where Abū Hāshim states that heaviness and lightness are real attributes that cause sinking and floating, with no effect from air.
Notice the strong claim: 'air has no effect at all'.
2
Understand the first objection
The first objection: iron sinks but a thin iron plate floats, even though the weight is the same. Why does this challenge the theory?
If heaviness alone caused sinking, the plate should sink too.
3
Understand the second objection
Second objection: a grain of iron sinks while a thousand tons of wood floats. This shows relative weight is not the sole determinant.
The weight difference is huge, yet the heavier wood floats.
4
Consider the commentator's response
The commentator notes that the first objection also applies to al-Jubbā'ī, and that the objections may be answered by appealing to impediments (mawāni'). The effect of a requirer can be blocked.
This is a key concept in kalam causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابنه : ( أبو هاشم ( إنه للثقل والخفة ) أي الرسوب للثقل والطفو للخفة ( وهما ) أي الثقل والخفة ( أمران حقيقيان عارضان للجسم ) في نفسه ( كما مر) يقتضي أحدهما الرسوب والآخر الطفو ولا أثر للهواء في ذلك أصلاً . ( ويلزمه أمران الأول أن الحديد يرسب ) في الماء ( فإذا اتخذ منه صفيحة رقيقة طفا ) ذلك الحديد الذي جعل صفيحة على الماء ( مع أن الثقل في الحالين واحد ) فلو كان الثقل مطلقاً موجباً للرسوب لما اختلفا الثاني أنَّ حبة حديد ترسب) في الماء ( وألف طن خشباً لا يرسب ) فيه مع أنه لا نسبة لثقل الحبة إلى ثقل ألف طن. قوله : ( الأول أن الحديد إلخ يلزم هذا الأمر على الجبائي أيضاً. قوله: (مطلقاً) فيه إشارة إلى أن الجواب عنه بما نقل عن الحكماء من أن الاحتياج إلى تحية الماء الكثير يمنعه عن الرسوب خلاف ظاهر كلامه . قوله : ( يلزمه أمران إلخ) قد أشرنا إلى أنَّ اللازم الأول لأبي هاشم يلزم إياه أيضاً ثم إنّ حمل كلامه على أن الثقل مقتض للرسوب والخفة للطفو لم يرد هذا بل الثاني أيضاً لجواز التخلف عن المتقضي لمانع كما مر غير مرة .
And his son Abū Hāshim said: It is due to heaviness and lightness — i.e., sinking is due to heaviness and floating is due to lightness — and they (i.e., heaviness and lightness) are two real attributes that occur to the body in itself, as previously mentioned. One requires sinking and the other requires floating, and air has no effect in that at all. (And there follow for him) two matters: first, iron sinks in water, but when it is made into a thin plate it floats, even though the heaviness in both cases is one; so if heaviness absolutely necessitated sinking, they would not differ. Second, a grain of iron sinks in water, while a thousand tons of wood does not sink in it, even though there is no proportion between the weight of the grain and the weight of a thousand tons. His saying: ('The first is that iron, etc.') — this matter also follows for al-Jubbā'ī. His saying: ('Absolutely') — in it there is an indication that the answer to it based on what is transmitted from the philosophers about the need for the water's force preventing sinking is contrary to the apparent meaning of his statement. His saying: ('There follow for him two matters, etc.') — we have indicated that the first necessary consequence for Abū Hāshim also follows for him, and then if his statement is interpreted as heaviness being a requirer of sinking and lightness of floating, this is not refuted; rather, the second also [is not refuted] because of the possibility of the effect failing to occur due to an impediment, as has been mentioned many times.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents Abū Hāshim's theory that sinking and floating are caused by two real attributes inherent in bodies: heaviness (thiqal) for sinking and lightness (khiffa) for floating, with no role for air. Two objections are raised against this view. First, iron sinks in water as a solid lump, but when beaten into a thin plate it floats, even though the weight is the same. If heaviness alone necessitated sinking, the plate should also sink. Second, a tiny grain of iron sinks, whereas a huge mass of wood (a thousand tons) floats, showing that relative weight is not the sole determinant. The commentator then notes that the first objection also applies to al-Jubbā'ī's theory, and that the phrase 'absolutely' (muṭlaqan) in the objection hints at a possible answer from the philosophers about water resistance, but this contradicts Abū Hāshim's apparent meaning. The commentator further explains that the objections do not necessarily refute Abū Hāshim because a requirer (muqtaḍī) may be impeded by a hindrance, as repeatedly stated. Thus the theory remains plausible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objections decisively prove Abū Hāshim's theory is false.
Correction: The commentator argues that a requirer may be impeded, so the theory is not refuted by these counterexamples.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection against Abū Hāshim's theory?
A) Gold sinks in mercury but silver floats.
B) Iron sinks but a thin iron plate floats despite equal weight.
C) Wood floats but a grain of iron sinks.
D) Air has no effect on floating.

Key Takeaway
Abū Hāshim's theory that heaviness and lightness cause sinking and floating faces two empirical objections, but the commentator defends it by appealing to possible impediments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a time when you were certain that a cause would produce a particular effect, but it did not. How did that challenge your understanding of how Allah's system of causes and impediments works? What can you learn from Abū Hāshim's theory and the commentator's defense about maintaining faith in divine wisdom despite apparent contradictions?
?
The objections in this lesson seem to refute Abū Hāshim, yet the commentator argues they do not because of possible impediments. Is there a belief you hold about Islam that appears contradicted by evidence or experience? What unseen impediments might account for the discrepancy, and how can you strengthen your trust in Allah's perfect system?
?
You have just read that heaviness and lightness are real attributes, but their effects can be blocked. Reflect on your own life: what 'heaviness' (spiritual or personal) do you assume will inevitably lead to a certain outcome, only to find it does not? How does the concept of a māniʿ (impediment) help you accept Allah's decree with patience and trust?

The Philosophers' Explanation of Floating and Sinking
Philosophers explain floating/sinking based on relative weight compared to water volume.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of floating and sinking based on the relative weight of a body compared to an equal volume of water.

Core Idea
A body’s buoyancy depends on its weight relative to the weight of an equal volume of water.

The philosophers (al-ḥukamā') offer a precise physical explanation of why objects float or sink. Their account, presented here as a branch of a broader Mu'tazilite discussion, reduces buoyancy to a simple comparison of weights. This lesson explores the three cases they describe.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the branch (far‘)
Read the opening lines to see that the philosophers’ view is brought in as a digression (tafrī‘) from the main Mu‘tazilite discussion. The author clarifies that 'branching' here means secondary mention, not a formal derivation.
The term 'tafrī‘' is used in a special sense, so pay attention to the commentary.
2
Learn the three cases: heavy, equal, and light
Identify the three conditions—body heavier than water, equal in weight, or lighter—and their respective outcomes: sinking, partial immersion, and floating with a precise ratio.
Equal weight case results in the body’s upper surface coinciding with the water’s surface.
3
Grasp the quantitative condition for partial submersion
For a lighter body, the immersed part displaces water weighing exactly the same as the whole body. The commentary provides a numerical example: if immersed part is half the external part, it is one third of the total, and the body’s weight is one third of the water’s weight.
This establishes a proportional relationship central to the theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللحكماء كلام يناسب ما ذهب إليه أبو هاشم فأورده هاهنا وجعله فرعاً بناء على أن المقصود الأصلي من المبحث السادس بيان اختلافات المعتزلة في الاعتمادات فإيراد كلام غيرهم فيه إنما يكون على سبيل التبعية والفرعية فلذلك قال : (تفريع. قال الحكماء الجسم إن كان أثقل من الماء على تقدير تساويهما في الحجم ( رسب ) ذلك الجسم ( فيه ) لأنه بثقله الزائد على ثقل الماء يغلب عليه ويخرق ما يلاقيه منه وينزل فيه ) إلى تحت وإن كان الجسم مع مساواته للماء في الحجم ( مثله في الثقل نزل فيه بحيث يماس سطحه الأعلى السطح الأعلى من الماء ) فلا يكون طافيا عليه ولا راسباً رسوباً تاماً ( وإن كان ( الجسم مع التساوي في الحجم ( أخف منه ) أي من (نزل فيه بعضه وذلك ( البعض النازل يكون بقدر ما لو ملئ مكانه ماء كان ) ذلك الماء الذي ملئ به مکانه (موازنا ) و مساويا في الثقل ) لذلك الجسم كله فيكون نسبة القدر النازل منه في الماء إلى القدر الباقي ( منه في خارجه كنسبة ثقل ذلك الجسم . قوله : ( إنما يكون على سبيل (التبعية يعني ليس التفريع هاهنا بالمعنى المتعارف وهو ترتيب حكم جزئي على حكم كلي بل بمعنى ذكر الشيء على سبيل التبعية والاستطراد . قوله : ( وينزل فيه ويصل إلى الأرض إن لم يمنعه مانع وإلا وقف حيث منع. قوله : ( نزل فيه بحيث يماس إلخ ) لأنه يقتضي بطبعه أن يكون حيزه حيز الماء . قوله : ( ويكون نسبة القدر النازل إلخ ) توضيحه أن يقال: إذا فرضنا أن القدر النازل نصف القدر الخارج يكون النازل ثلث المجموع فيكون ثقل مجموع الجسم ثلث ثقل الماء المساوي.
And the philosophers have a statement that suits what Abū Hāshim held, so he (the author) mentioned it here and made it a branch (farʿ), based on the fact that the primary purpose of the sixth investigation is to explain the differences of the Muʿtazila concerning iʿtimādāt (dependencies), so mentioning the statement of others in it is only by way of derivation and subordination. Therefore he said: (A branching). The philosophers said: If a body is heavier than water on the assumption of equality in volume, it sinks in it because by its excess weight over the weight of water it overcomes it and tears through what it meets from it and descends into it to the bottom. If the body is equal in weight to water while equal in volume, it descends in it such that its upper surface contacts the upper surface of the water, so it is neither floating on it nor completely sinking. And if the body is lighter than water while equal in volume, part of it descends in it, and that descending part is such that if its place were filled with water, that water would be equal in weight to the whole body. So the ratio of the descending part to the remaining part outside is like the ratio of the weight of that body. His saying: ('Only by way of derivation') — meaning that ‘branching’ (tafrīʿ) here is not in the conventional sense of arranging a particular ruling under a universal ruling, but in the sense of mentioning something by way of derivation and digression. His saying: ('And descends in it and reaches the ground unless an impediment prevents it, otherwise it stops where prevented.') His saying: ('Descends in it such that its upper surface contacts, etc.') — because it necessarily requires by nature that its space be the space of water. His saying: ('And the ratio of the descending part, etc.') — its clarification is: if we suppose that the descending part is half of the external part, then the descending part is one third of the total, so the weight of the total body is one third of the weight of the equal water.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (al-ḥukamā') explanation of floating and sinking, which the author includes as a subordinate branch (farʿ) to the Muʿtazilite discussion. The author notes that the primary aim of the sixth investigation is the differences among the Muʿtazila regarding iʿtimādāt (accidental dependencies), so the philosophers' view is brought in only secondarily. The philosophers' theory is based on relative density: if a body is heavier than an equal volume of water, it sinks because its extra weight overcomes the water. If it is equal in weight to an equal volume of water, it suspends at the surface, neither floating nor fully sinking. If it is lighter, it floats with part immersed, such that the displaced water's weight equals the body's total weight. The commentator clarifies that 'branching' here does not mean subsuming a particular under a universal but rather a digression. He also explains that the body's descent continues until impeded, and that the equal-weight case results in the body's upper surface coinciding with water's surface. Finally, he illustrates the ratio: if the immersed part is half the volume above, then the immersed part is one third of the total, and the body's weight is one third of the weight of the displaced water.

Key Terms
الحكماء
الثقل
الحجم
الطفو
الرسوب
التساوي
التفريع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers' view contradicts Abū Hāshim or is a main part of the Mu'tazilite discussion.
Correction: The philosophers' view actually complements the discussion and is presented only as a secondary branch (farʿ). The primary focus remains on Mu'tazilite differences regarding i'timādāt.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what happens when a body has the same weight as an equal volume of water?
A) It sinks completely.
B) It floats completely.
C) It descends such that its upper surface contacts the water's surface.
D) It oscillates up and down.

Key Takeaway
Floating and sinking are determined by whether a body’s weight is greater than, equal to, or less than the weight of an equal volume of water.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a massive ship floating, do you instinctively attribute it to physical laws like these, or do you sense the continuous divine act that sustains it? How can you train your heart to see the Creator behind the created order?
?
The philosophers' explanation is presented as a branch, secondary to the core theological discussion. In your daily life, where do you tend to focus on secondary causes (the 'how') rather than the primary cause (God)? What is one specific habit you can change today to redirect your attention to Allah as the ultimate actor in every event?

Two Objections Against Absolute Weight Theory in Buoyancy
Two objections against absolute weight theory in buoyancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two objections from Islamic theology (kalam) that demonstrate why absolute weight cannot be the sole cause of sinking or floating in water.

Core Idea
Weight alone does not determine whether an object sinks or floats.

This passage presents two objections from the classical Islamic theological work 'Sharh al-Mawaqif' against the view that sinking is determined purely by weight. The first objection uses the example of iron: a solid lump sinks, but a thin sheet of the same weight floats. The second contrasts a small grain of iron with a large mass of wood: the heavy iron sinks while the much heavier wood floats. Both examples show that factors like shape and density matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two objections are clearly numbered.
2
Identify the first objection
Find the first objection about iron sinking as a lump but floating as a sheet. Note that weight is the same, so weight cannot be the only cause.
3
Identify the second objection
Find the second objection comparing a grain of iron to a thousand tons of wood. The wood is heavier but floats, so weight alone is not decisive.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this passage tells us about the method of kalam: using empirical examples to test theoretical claims.
Kalam is not just abstract reasoning; it engages with observable phenomena.

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل. أمران : الأول أن الحديد يرسب في الماء فإذا اتخذ منه صفيحة رقيقة طفا ذلك الحديد الذي جعل صفيحة على الماء مع أن الثقل في الحالين واحد، فلو كان الثقل مطلقاً موجباً للرسوب لما اختلفا. الثاني أن حبة حديد ترسب في الماء وألف طن خشباً لا يرسب فيه مع أنه لا نسبة لثقل الحبة إلى ثقل ألف طن.
The third observation point - the third objective: on reliance (al-iʿtimād) which is inclination (al-mayl). Two matters: First, that iron sinks in water, but if one makes from it a thin sheet, that iron which was made a sheet floats on water, while the weight in both cases is one. So if weight absolutely necessitated sinking, they would not differ. Second, that a grain of iron sinks in water, but a thousand tons of wood does not sink in it, while there is no proportion between the weight of the grain and the weight of a thousand tons.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the view that absolute weight determines whether a body sinks or floats in water. The author states that this is part of the third observation point on the topic of 'reliance' or 'inclination'. The first objection: iron sinks as a solid lump, but if beaten into a thin sheet, it floats even though the weight is the same. If weight alone caused sinking, the behavior would not differ. The second objection: a small grain of iron sinks, but a massive quantity of wood (a thousand tons) floats. Since the weight of the grain is negligible compared to the wood, weight cannot be the sole cause. These objections show that other factors, such as shape and density relative to the medium, must be considered.

The Three Categories of Bodies in Water
Classification of bodies relative to water into three types with rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of bodies in water into three categories based on relative weight and volume, and the proportional relationship that governs flotation and sinking.

Core Idea
Buoyancy and sinking are determined by precise weight-to-volume ratios.

In this branch passage, the philosophers present a rational classification of how bodies behave when placed in water. This emerges from earlier objections about causality and natural motion. The classification relies on comparing the weight of the body with the weight of an equal volume of water, yielding three distinct outcomes—sinking, suspension, and partial flotation—each with a precise proportional relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Let the philosophers' own words establish the three cases before you analyze them.
2
Identify the three categories
Identify the three conditions (heavier, equal weight, lighter) and their corresponding behaviors (sink, suspend, partially float). Notice how each definition begins with 'if the body...'
3
Examine the ratio for the lighter body
Focus on the sentence describing the ratio for the lighter body: the submerged part to the exposed part equals the body's weight to the 'excess weight of water' (the difference between the water's weight and the body's weight).
This ratio is key to understanding the causal relationship.
4
Reflect on the analogical reasoning
Consider the author's comment that the cause for the second and third categories is learned by analogy from the first (heavier) case. Ask yourself how the same principle of weight comparison extends to all three cases.
Why does the author say 'so reflect' at the end?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفريع. قال الحكماء : الجسم إن كان أثقل من الماء على تقدير تساويهما في الحجم رسب ذلك الجسم فيه لأنه بثقله الزائد على ثقل الماء يغلب عليه ويخرق ما يلاقيه منه وينزل فيه إلى تحت. وإن كان الجسم مع مساواته للماء في الحجم مثله في الثقل نزل فيه بحيث يماس سطحه الأعلى السطح الأعلى من الماء فلا يكون طافياً عليه ولا راسباً رسوباً تاماً. وإن كان الجسم مع التساوي في الحجم أخف منه نزل فيه بعضه وذلك البعض النازل يكون بقدر ما لو ملئ مكانه ماء كان ذلك الماء الذي ملئ به مكانه موازناً ومساوياً في الثقل لذلك الجسم كله، فيكون نسبة القدر النازل منه في الماء إلى القدر الباقي منه في خارجه كنسبة ثقل ذلك الجسم إلى فضل ثقل الماء علة الحكم في هذين القسمين تعلم بالمقايسة على القسم الأول فتأمل.
Branch. The philosophers (al-ḥukamāʾ) said: If a body is heavier than water, assuming equality in volume, that body sinks in it because by its excess weight over the weight of water it overpowers it, penetrates what it meets of it, and descends into it to the bottom. And if the body, while equal in volume to water, is equal in weight, it descends into it such that its upper surface touches the upper surface of the water, so it is neither floating upon it nor sinking completely. And if the body, while equal in volume, is lighter than it, part of it descends into it, and that descending part is such that if its place were filled with water, that water which filled its place would be balancing and equal in weight to that whole body. So the ratio of the part descending in water to the part remaining outside it is like the ratio of the weight of that body to the excess weight of water. The cause of the judgment in these two categories is learned by analogy from the first category, so reflect.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation by the philosophers (ḥukamāʾ) regarding the behavior of bodies in water. It is presented as a branch (tafrīʿ) following the objections. The philosophers consider three cases based on the relative weight and volume of the body compared to water: 1) Heavier: if the body is heavier than an equal volume of water, it sinks completely because its excess weight overcomes the water. 2) Equal: if the body is exactly as heavy as an equal volume of water, it is fully submerged but does not sink to the bottom; it remains suspended with its upper surface level with the water's surface. 3) Lighter: if the body is lighter than an equal volume of water, it floats partially submerged. The submerged part displaces exactly enough water to equal the body's total weight. The author then gives the ratio: the submerged part to the exposed part is proportional to the body's weight to the excess weight of water (the difference between the water's weight and the body's weight? Actually the text says 'the excess weight of water' meaning the amount by which the weight of an equal volume of water exceeds the body's weight). The cause for the two categories (equal and lighter?) is understood by analogy to the first (heavier) case. The author ends with 'fa-taʾammal' (so reflect), urging careful thought.

Why Flat Iron Floats and Wood Rises: Reconciling Philosophers and Kalam
Philosophers' explanation of floating and lightness, with reconciliation of Abu Hashim's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical explanations given by philosophers for floating and lightness in solids, and how these explanations can reconcile the position of Abu Hashim with natural observations.

Core Idea
Floating is not just about weight—it's about water displacement and trapped air.

The philosophers offered concrete reasons for why flat iron does not sink and why wood floats. These explanations involve concepts like water displacement and the natural inclination of air. The author then shows how these physical accounts can be used to defend Abu Hashim's views against earlier objections. This lesson unpacks those arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice the two distinct explanations: one for flat iron and one for wood.
2
Identify the two physical mechanisms
For flat iron: it needs to displace a large amount of water, which resists. For wood: trapped air inside has a natural upward inclination when in water, pushing the wood upward if strong enough.
The air's inclination is key to understanding lightness in porous solids.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The author concludes that if Abu Hashim's words are interpreted according to these philosopher explanations, the earlier objections against him are repelled. Reflect on how the examples illustrate the principles.
This shows that kalam and natural philosophy can be compatible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنهم قالوا: إن الحديدة المنبسطة إنما لا تنزل في الماء لاحتياجها إلى أن ينحي من تحتها ماء كثير وذلك لا يطاوعها بخلاف الحديدة المدورة. وقالوا أيضاً إن سبب الخفة في الأجرام الصلبة تخلخل الهواء فيما بينها، فالخشبة مثلاً إذا كانت في الهواء لم يكن للأجزاء الهوائية المتخلخلة فيها ميل، فإذا وقعت في الماء انبعث الميل الطبيعي للهواء إلى فوق، فإن قوي وقاوم الأجزاء الثقيلة دفع الخشبة إلى فوق، وإن لم يقو على ذلك إذ عن للهبوط قسر إن لم يتأت له الانفصال عنها. وبما قررناه ظهر لك أنه إن حمل كلام أبي هاشم على ما قاله الحكماء اندفع.
And know that they said: The flat iron only does not descend in water because it needs to displace a large amount of water from beneath it, and that does not comply with it, unlike the round iron. And they also said: The cause of lightness in solid bodies is the rarefaction of air between their parts. For example, wood, when it is in the air, the rarefied air particles in it have no inclination; but when it falls into water, the natural inclination of air is excited upward. If it is strong and resists the heavy parts, it pushes the wood upward; and if it is not strong enough for that, then [the wood] is compelled to descend, unless separation [of the air] from it can occur. And by what we have established, it has become clear to you that if the words of Abū Hāshim are interpreted according to what the philosophers said, the objection is repelled.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage provides two further explanations and a reconciliation. First, it explains why a flat iron sheet does not sink: it requires displacing a large amount of water from underneath, which offers resistance; a rounded iron does not have this issue and sinks. Second, it explains the cause of lightness in solid bodies like wood: the presence of rarefied air within their pores. In air, this trapped air has no natural inclination (since it is in its natural place), but when the wood enters water, the air's natural inclination (to rise) activates. If the upward force of the air is strong enough to overcome the weight of the solid parts, it pushes the wood upward (floating). If not, the wood sinks unless the air can escape. Finally, the author concludes that by these clarifications, if one interprets Abū Hāshim's statements about weight and lightness in accordance with the philosophers' relative-weight view, then the earlier objections against him are repelled. Thus, the passage resolves the apparent conflict between Abū Hāshim's position and the physical evidence.

تخلخل الهواء
ميل طبيعي
أبو هاشم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Floating or sinking is solely determined by the density of the object.
Correction: The passage shows that for flat iron, the shape and need to displace water matter, and for wood, trapped air with upward inclination can cause floating even if the wood is denser than water (e.g., buoyancy from air pockets).

The Shape Theory of Flotation and Sinking
Explains shape as a cause of flotation/sinking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the shape of a solid object affects its ability to float or sink, according to the theory based on water displacement.

Core Idea
A flat iron sinks because it cannot displace enough water; a round iron can.

This lesson introduces the shape-based explanation for flotation and sinking. The text contrasts a flat iron bar, which sinks because it cannot push aside enough water, with a round iron bar, which floats more easily. This theory highlights the role of water displacement in buoyancy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on shape
Read the Arabic passage about the flat and round iron. Note how the flat iron's inability to displace water is given as the reason.
The passage begins with the definition of reliance (i'timad) but focus on the shape example.
2
Understand the comparison
The commentator explains 'so reflect' – compare with the first category. Sinking is due to excess weight, floating due to lightness, and equality leads to neutral buoyancy.
3
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: Why does shape matter? The key is the amount of water that needs to be displaced. A flat surface requires more force to push water aside.
This connects to the concept of resistance from water.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل إلى فضل ثقل الماء علة الحكم في هذين القسمين تعلم بالمقايسة على القسم الأول فتأمل . واعلم أنهم قالوا : إن الحديدة المنبسطة، إنما لا تنزل في الماء لاحتياجها إلى أن ينحي من تحتها ماء كثير وذلك لا يطاوعها بخلاف الحديدة المدورة، وقالوا أيضاً إِنَّ سبب الخفة في الأجرام الصلبة تخلخل الهواء فيما بينها فالخشبة مثلاً إذا كانت في الهواء لم يكن للأجزاء الهوائية المتخلخلة فيها ميل، فإذا وقعت في الماء انبعث الميل الطبيعي للهواء إلى فوق فإن قوي وقاوم الأجزاء الثقيلة دفع الخشبة إلى فوق وإن لم يقو على ذلك إذ عن للهبوط قسر إن لم يتأت له الانفصال عنها. قوله : ( في هذين القسمين) أي الأثقل والأخف . قوله : (فتأمل) أي في المقايسة وهو أنه لما كان الرسوب بسبب زيادة الثقل كان في صورة المساواة في موضع الماء ملاقياً بسطحه سطحه وفي صورة الخفة كان طافياً بقدر الخفة وراسباً بقدر ما يساوي ثقل الماء. قوله : ( في الأجرام الصلبة وأما الأجسام اللينة فالسبب فيها مزاجها المقتضي للخفة كما البسائط. قوله : ( لم يكن للأجزاء الهوائية إلخ) لانعدام الميل عند الحصول في الحيز الطبيعي. قوله : ( إن لم يتأت له الانفصال إلخ) وإن تأتي انفصلت وبقي ما عداها راسب في الماء.
The third observatory - the third objective: On reliance (al-i'timad), which is inclination (al-mayl) to the excess of the weight of water is the cause of the judgment in these two categories; you learn it by comparison (al-muqāyasa) with the first category, so reflect. And know that they said: The flat iron bar does not descend in water because it needs to displace a large amount of water from beneath it, and that does not yield to it, unlike the round iron bar. And they also said: The cause of lightness in solid bodies is the rarefaction (takhalkhul) of air within them. For example, when wood is in the air, the rarefied air particles within it have no inclination (mayl). When it falls into water, the natural inclination of air upward is activated. If it is strong and overcomes the heavy parts, it pushes the wood upward. If it is not strong enough for that, then it is forced to descend by compulsion (qasr) if it cannot separate from it. His statement: 'in these two categories' means the heavier and the lighter. His statement: 'so reflect' means in the comparison: since sinking occurs due to increase of weight, in the case of equality, the body contacts the water surface with its surface, and in the case of lightness, it floats to the extent of lightness and sinks to the extent that equals the weight of water. His statement: 'in solid bodies' and as for soft bodies, the cause in them is their temperament necessitating lightness like the simple bodies. His statement: 'the rarefied air particles had no inclination' due to the absence of inclination when it is in its natural place. His statement: 'if it cannot separate from it' — and if it can separate, it separates and the rest remains sinking in the water.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage defines the third objective of the third observation, dealing with reliance (al-i'timad) which is inclination (al-mayl). It explains two theories for the phenomena of flotation and sinking. The first theory attributes sinking of a flat iron to its inability to displace sufficient water, unlike a round one. The second theory explains the lightness of solid bodies like wood by the rarefaction of air within them. When such a body enters water, the air inside, being naturally inclined to rise, pushes the body upward if it is strong enough; otherwise, the body sinks by compulsion. The commentator then explains key phrases: 'in these two categories' refers to the heavier and lighter bodies; 'so reflect' points to the comparison: sinking is due to excess weight, floating is due to lightness, and equality leads to neutral buoyancy. The commentator also clarifies that in solid bodies, the cause is the rarefied air, while for soft bodies, it is their temperament. The explanation of 'no inclination' for the air particles is because they are in their natural place, and the separation condition indicates that if the air can detach, the remaining part sinks.

The Rarefaction of Air as a Cause of Buoyancy
Explains rarefaction of air as cause of buoyancy in solids.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rarefaction of air within solid bodies can cause them to float by the upward natural inclination of air when immersed in water.

Core Idea
Wood floats because trapped air has a natural upward inclination.

This lesson presents the second theory for flotation: the rarefaction of air inside solid bodies. When a body like wood enters water, the air within it, being naturally inclined to rise, pushes the body upward if strong enough, otherwise the body sinks by compulsion. The commentator also clarifies that for soft bodies, the cause is their temperament.

Guided Path
1
Read the rarefaction theory
Read the passage about the example of wood and the behavior of rarefied air. Notice the condition: if the air is strong, it pushes the wood up; if not, it is forced to sink.
The passage begins with 'And they also said'.
2
Understand the role of natural inclination
The air inside has a natural inclination upward when in water. This is because water is not its natural place.
The commentator notes that in air, there is no inclination because it is in its natural place.
3
Distinguish soft bodies
The commentator mentions that for soft bodies, the cause of lightness is their temperament (mizāj), not rarefied air.
This is a contrast to solid bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل إلى فضل ثقل الماء علة الحكم في هذين القسمين تعلم بالمقايسة على القسم الأول فتأمل . واعلم أنهم قالوا : إن الحديدة المنبسطة، إنما لا تنزل في الماء لاحتياجها إلى أن ينحي من تحتها ماء كثير وذلك لا يطاوعها بخلاف الحديدة المدورة، وقالوا أيضاً إِنَّ سبب الخفة في الأجرام الصلبة تخلخل الهواء فيما بينها فالخشبة مثلاً إذا كانت في الهواء لم يكن للأجزاء الهوائية المتخلخلة فيها ميل، فإذا وقعت في الماء انبعث الميل الطبيعي للهواء إلى فوق فإن قوي وقاوم الأجزاء الثقيلة دفع الخشبة إلى فوق وإن لم يقو على ذلك إذ عن للهبوط قسر إن لم يتأت له الانفصال عنها. قوله : ( في هذين القسمين) أي الأثقل والأخف . قوله : (فتأمل) أي في المقايسة وهو أنه لما كان الرسوب بسبب زيادة الثقل كان في صورة المساواة في موضع الماء ملاقياً بسطحه سطحه وفي صورة الخفة كان طافياً بقدر الخفة وراسباً بقدر ما يساوي ثقل الماء. قوله : ( في الأجرام الصلبة وأما الأجسام اللينة فالسبب فيها مزاجها المقتضي للخفة كما البسائط. قوله : ( لم يكن للأجزاء الهوائية إلخ) لانعدام الميل عند الحصول في الحيز الطبيعي. قوله : ( إن لم يتأت له الانفصال إلخ) وإن تأتي انفصلت وبقي ما عداها راسب في الماء.
The third observatory - the third objective: On reliance (al-i'timad), which is inclination (al-mayl) to the excess of the weight of water is the cause of the judgment in these two categories; you learn it by comparison (al-muqāyasa) with the first category, so reflect. And know that they said: The flat iron bar does not descend in water because it needs to displace a large amount of water from beneath it, and that does not yield to it, unlike the round iron bar. And they also said: The cause of lightness in solid bodies is the rarefaction (takhalkhul) of air within them. For example, when wood is in the air, the rarefied air particles within it have no inclination (mayl). When it falls into water, the natural inclination of air upward is activated. If it is strong and overcomes the heavy parts, it pushes the wood upward. If it is not strong enough for that, then it is forced to descend by compulsion (qasr) if it cannot separate from it. His statement: 'in these two categories' means the heavier and the lighter. His statement: 'so reflect' means in the comparison: since sinking occurs due to increase of weight, in the case of equality, the body contacts the water surface with its surface, and in the case of lightness, it floats to the extent of lightness and sinks to the extent that equals the weight of water. His statement: 'in solid bodies' and as for soft bodies, the cause in them is their temperament necessitating lightness like the simple bodies. His statement: 'the rarefied air particles had no inclination' due to the absence of inclination when it is in its natural place. His statement: 'if it cannot separate from it' — and if it can separate, it separates and the rest remains sinking in the water.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage defines the third objective of the third observation, dealing with reliance (al-i'timad) which is inclination (al-mayl). It explains two theories for the phenomena of flotation and sinking. The first theory attributes sinking of a flat iron to its inability to displace sufficient water, unlike a round one. The second theory explains the lightness of solid bodies like wood by the rarefaction of air within them. When such a body enters water, the air inside, being naturally inclined to rise, pushes the body upward if it is strong enough; otherwise, the body sinks by compulsion. The commentator then explains key phrases: 'in these two categories' refers to the heavier and lighter bodies; 'so reflect' points to the comparison: sinking is due to excess weight, floating is due to lightness, and equality leads to neutral buoyancy. The commentator also clarifies that in solid bodies, the cause is the rarefied air, while for soft bodies, it is their temperament. The explanation of 'no inclination' for the air particles is because they are in their natural place, and the separation condition indicates that if the air can detach, the remaining part sinks.

تخلخل الهواء (takhalkhul al-hawā’)
الميل الطبيعي (al-mayl al-ṭabī‘ī)
القسر (al-qasr)
الأجرام الصلبة (al-ajrām al-ṣulba)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All floating objects float because they are lighter than water.
Correction: The passage shows that rarefied air inside a body can provide an upward force, even if the body itself is heavy. The air's natural inclination is the key.

Rarefied air inside solid bodies, when activated by water, can cause flotation through its natural upward inclination.

Or are they only activated when you are displaced by difficulty? If you find you only turn to Allah when you are in distress, what does that say about the state of your heart? Will you commit to nurturing that upward inclination even when surrounded by tranquility?

The passage mentions that if the air can separate from the solid, the remainder sinks. Reconciling Abu Hashim with the Philosophers on Buoyancy
Shows proportional submergence from reconciled interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hashim's statement on weight and buoyancy can be reconciled with the philosophers' view through a proportional rule of submergence.

Core Idea
Sinking depth is determined by the ratio of the body's weight to the water's weight.

This passage addresses a critical objection against Abu Hashim's theory of weight. The commentator shows that if we interpret Abu Hashim's terms in line with the philosophers—considering weight relative to an equal volume of water—then a clear proportional relationship emerges. This resolves the objections and provides a consistent account of buoyancy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the claim that interpretation repels objections.
The text contains a possible corruption: 'اندفع الخارج' may be 'اندفع الاعتراضان'.
2
Identify the proportional relationship
The passage states that if the body's weight is one-third of the water's weight, one-third of the body submerges. This is the core of the reconciliation.
The ratio of submerged to external parts matches the ratio of body weight to excess water weight.
3
Generalize the rule
The text says 'and measure accordingly', implying a general law: submerged fraction equals body weight divided by water weight (for equal volumes).
For example, half the weight means half submerges.
4
Understand the clarification on inclination
The commentator clarifies that 'mayl' (inclination) here means repulsion (mudāfa‘a), and its origin is not disproven.
This is part of the broader defense of Abu Hashim's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبما قررناه ظهر لك أنه إن حمل كلام أبي هاشم على ما قاله الحكماء اندفع الخارج يكون النازل ثلث المجموع فيكون ثقل مجموع الجسم ثلث ثقل الماء المساوي له في الحج ونسبة ثقل الجسم إلى فضل ثقل الماء بالنصفية كما أنَّ نسبة القدر النازل إلى القدر الخارج كذلك وقس على ذلك . قوله : ( وبما قررناه في حل عبارة المتن وقوله : واعلم إلخ . قوله : ( إن حمل كلام أبي هاشم إلخ بأن لا يراد بالثقل والخفة مطلقهما كما هو الظاهر من له في الحجم ونسبة ثقل الجسم إلى فضل ثقل الماء بالتصفية كما أن نسبة القدر النازل إلى القدر الخارج كذلك ولما كان النازل تقدير تساوي الثقلين تمام الجسم من غير رسوب تام ولا طفو تام كان النازل على تقدير كون ثقل الجسم نصف ثقل الماء نصف الجسم وثلثه على تقدير كون ثقله ثلث ثقل الماء وعلى هذا القياس. قوله : (لم يكن للأجزاء الهوائية المتخلخلة فيها ميل أراد بالميل المدافعة إذ قد سبق المعدوم في الجسم إذا كان في الحيز الطبيعي هو المدافعة وأما مبدؤها فلا دليل على انتفائه حينئذ . قوله : ( على ما قاله الحكماء) وهو اعتبار الثقل والخفة بالنسبة إلى الماء المساوي للجسم في الحجم كما ذهب إليه بعض الحكماء من أنَّ الأشياء كلها تميل إلى مركز العالم .
And by what we have established, it becomes clear to you that if the statement of Abu Hashim is interpreted according to what the philosophers said, then the external is repelled: the submerged part is one-third of the whole, so the weight of the whole body is one-third of the weight of water equal to it in volume, and the ratio of the body's weight to the excess weight of water is by half, just as the ratio of the submerged part to the external part is like that. And measure accordingly. His statement: 'and by what we have established' in resolving the expression of the text and his statement 'and know' etc. His statement: 'if the statement of Abu Hashim is interpreted' in that weight and lightness are not meant in their absolute senses, as is apparent from him, in the volume, and the ratio of the body's weight to the excess weight of water is by half, just as the ratio of the submerged part to the external part is like that. And since the submerged part is estimated by the equality of the two weights without complete sinking or complete floating, the submerged part is, on the estimation that the body's weight is half the weight of water, half the body; and one-third on the estimation that its weight is one-third of the weight of water, and so on. His statement: 'the rarefied air particles had no inclination' — he meant by inclination the repulsion (al-mudāfa‘a), for it has already been stated that the non-existent in the body when it is in its natural place is the repulsion, and as for its origin, there is no proof of its non-existence at that time. His statement: 'according to what the philosophers said' — that is, considering weight and lightness relative to the water equal to the body in volume, as some philosophers held that all things tend towards the center of the world.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage shows how to reconcile the statement of Abu Hashim (a Mu'tazilite) with the view of the philosophers (al-ḥukamā') regarding buoyancy. The author claims that if Abu Hashim's words are interpreted in line with the philosophers, the two objections against him are repelled. The central idea is that the proportion of a body that submerges is determined by the ratio of its weight to the weight of an equal volume of water. For example, if the body's weight is half that of the water, half of it submerges; if one-third, one-third submerges. The commentator explains that 'weight and lightness' are not meant absolutely but relative to the water. He also clarifies that the 'inclination' (mayl) in the previous context refers to repulsion (mudāfa‘a), and that its origin is not disproven. The philosophers' view is that all things tend towards the center of the world, so weight and lightness are relative to the medium.

الحكماء (al-ḥukamā')
الثقل (al-thiql)
الخفة (al-khiffa)
فضل ثقل الماء (faḍl thiqil al-mā')
النصفية (al-niṣfiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Abu Hashim's view on weight and buoyancy is entirely incompatible with the philosophers' account.
Correction: The passage shows that if Abu Hashim's terms are interpreted relatively (weight compared to equal volume water), his statements align with the philosophers' proportional law of submergence.

A) One-third
B) One-half
C) Two-thirds
D) The whole body

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's statement on weight can be reconciled with the philosophers' view by interpreting weight relative to an equal volume of water, leading to a proportional law of submergence.
